We talked to experts to discover the risks of catching the disease mid-flight.","IsSyndicated":true,"Location":null,"Partner":null,"PrimaryVertical":"wwfuture","Programme":null,"RelatedStory":[],"RelatedTag":null,"StoryType":"image","SummaryLong":"BBC Future talked to experts to find out what are the risks that the deadly virus could spread to other countries through air travel.","SummaryShort":"Experts offer their advice","Tag":[]},"Metadata":{"CreationDateTime":"2014-11-18T09:14:24Z","Entity":"story","Guid":"22da1f37-88ff-4850-8a44-823c8d58072d","Id":"wwfuture/story/20140731-can-ebola-spread-on-planes","ModifiedDateTime":"2014-11-18T09:14:24Z","Project":"wwfuture","Slug":"20140731-can-ebola-spread-on-planes"},"Urn":"urn:pubstack:jative:story:wwfuture/story/20140731-can-ebola-spread-on-planes","_id":"559a563a0b808738c4e2f8cb"},{"Content":{"AssetCustom":"","AssetIbroadcast":null,"AssetImage":[],"AssetImagePromo":null,"AssetInfographic":"","AssetInline":[],"AssetSelect":"","AssetVideoIb2":null,"AssetVideoMps":null,"Author":[],"BodyHtml":"&lt;p&gt;I have some startling news: you are not human. At least, by some counts. While you are indeed made up of billions of human cells working in remarkable concert, these are easily outnumbered by &lt;a href=\"http://www.bbc.com/future/story/20120412-the-beasts-inside-you\"&gt;the bacterial cells that live on and in you&lt;/a&gt; &ndash; your microbiome. There are ten of them for every one of your own cells, and they add an extra two kilograms (4.4lbs) to your body.&nbsp;&lt;/p&gt;&lt;p&gt;Far from being freeloading passengers, many of these microbes actively help digest food and prevent infection. And now evidence is emerging that these tiny organisms may also have a profound impact on the brain too. They are a living augmentation of your body &ndash; and like any enhancement, this means they could, in principle, be upgraded. So, could you hack your microbiome to make yourself healthier, happier, and smarter too?&lt;/p&gt;&lt;p&gt;According to John Cryan, this isn&rsquo;t as far-fetched as it sounds. As a professor of anatomy and neuroscience at University College Cork, he specialises in the relationship between the brain and the gut. One of his early experiments showed the diversity of bacteria living in the gut was greatly diminished in mice suffering from early life stress. This finding inspired him to investigate the connection between the microbiome and the brain.&lt;/p&gt;&lt;p&gt;The bacterial microbiota in the gut helps normal brain development, says Cryan. &ldquo;If you don&rsquo;t have microbiota you have major changes in brain structure and function, and then also in behaviour.&rdquo; In a pioneering study, a Japanese research team showed that mice raised without any gut bacteria had an exaggerated physical response to stress, releasing more hormone than mice that had a full complement of bacteria. However, this effect could be reduced in bacteria-free mice by repopulating their gut with &lt;em&gt;Bifidobacterium infantis&lt;/em&gt;, one of the major symbiotic bacteria found in the gut. Cryan&rsquo;s team built on this finding, showing that this effect could be reproduced even in healthy mice. &ldquo;We took healthy mice and fed them &lt;em&gt;Lactobacillus&lt;/em&gt; [another common gut bacteria), and we showed that these animals had a reduced stress response and reduced anxiety-related behaviours.&rdquo;&lt;/p&gt;&lt;p&gt;{\"image\":{\"pid\":\"p01slt3z\"}}&lt;/p&gt;&lt;p&gt;But why should bacteria in the gut affect the brain? There are several different ways that messages can be sent from one organ to the other. It can be hormones or immune cells via the bloodstream, or by impulses along the vagus nerve, which stretches from the brain to intertwine closely with the gut. Through these pathways, actions in one produce effects in the other.&lt;/p&gt;&lt;p&gt;So how might you go about altering your microbiome to do a spot of brain-hacking? Cryan&rsquo;s team works on several fronts, investigating the potential to manage stress, pain, obesity and cognition through the gut. &ldquo;We have unpublished data showing that probiotics can enhance learning in animal models,&rdquo; he tells me. His team tested the effects of two strains of bacteria, finding that one improved cognition in mice. His team is now embarking on human trials, to see if healthy volunteers can have their cognitive abilities enhanced or modulated by tweaking the gut microbiome.&lt;/p&gt;&lt;p&gt;Another method of adjusting the bacterial profile of your gut is to undergo a transplant that involves taking faecal material from a donor&rsquo;s intestine &ndash; often a close relative &ndash; and implanting into a recipient via enema infusion. This unorthodox treatment has been shown to successfully treat infections caused by pathogenic bacteria colonising the gut.&lt;/p&gt;&lt;p&gt;&lt;strong&gt;Brain boost&lt;/strong&gt;&lt;/p&gt;&lt;p&gt;Thankfully, Cryan has a far more appetising method on offer.&nbsp; &ldquo;Diet is perhaps the biggest factor in shaping the composition of the microbiome,&rdquo; he says. A study by University College Cork researchers &lt;a href=\"http://www.nature.com/nature/journal/v488/n7410/full/nature11319.html?WT.ec_id=NATURE-20120809\"&gt;published in Nature in 2012&lt;/a&gt; followed 200 elderly people over the course of two years, as they transitioned into different environments such as nursing homes. The researchers found that their subjects&rsquo; health &ndash; frailty, cognition, and immune system &ndash; all correlated with their microbiome. From bacterial population alone, researchers could tell if a patient was a long-stay patient in a nursing home, or short-stay, or living in the general community. These changes were a direct reflection of their diet in these different environments. &ldquo;A diverse diet gives you a diverse microbiome that gives you a better health outcome,&rdquo; says Cryan.&lt;/p&gt;&lt;p&gt;Beyond a healthy and varied diet, though, it still remains to be discovered whether certain food combinations could alter the microbiome to produce a cognitive boost. In fact, Cryan recommends that claims from probiotic supplements of brain-boosting ought to be taken with a pinch of salt for now. &ldquo;Unless the studies have been done, one can assume they&rsquo;re not going to have any effect on mental health,&rdquo; he says. Still, he&rsquo;s optimistic about the future. &ldquo;The field right now is evolving very strongly and quickly. There&rsquo;s a lot of important research to be done. It&rsquo;s still early days.&rdquo;&lt;/p&gt;&lt;p&gt;Hacking the brain often conjures up ideas of electrical hardware such as implants and trans-cranial stimulators. But it might be the case that a simple change in diet can shift your brain up a gear. The transhumanists and body hackers who believe that technology is the sole way to improve human ability would do well to pay as much attention to the living augmentation that already resides in their gut.&lt;/p&gt;&lt;p&gt;&lt;em&gt;If you would like to comment on this video or anything else you have seen on Future, head over to our &lt;/em&gt;&lt;a href=\"https://www.facebook.com/BBCFuture\"&gt;&lt;em&gt;Facebook&lt;/em&gt;&lt;/a&gt;&lt;em&gt; or &lt;/em&gt;&lt;a href=\"https://plus.google.com/u/0/b/107828172298602173375/107828172298602173375/posts\"&gt;&lt;em&gt;Google+&lt;/em&gt;&lt;/a&gt;&lt;em&gt; page, or message us on &lt;/em&gt;&lt;a href=\"https://twitter.com/BBC_Future\"&gt;&lt;em&gt;Twitter&lt;/em&gt;&lt;/a&gt;&lt;em&gt;.&lt;/em&gt;&lt;/p&gt;","BusinessUnit":"worldwide","CalloutBody":"","CalloutPosition":"","CalloutSubtitle":"","CalloutTitle":"","Campaign":null,"Collection":[],"DisableAdverts":false,"DisplayDate":"2014-02-21T00:00:00Z","HeadlineLong":"Body bacteria: Can your gut bugs make you smarter?","HeadlineShort":"Can gut bugs make you smarter?","HideRelated":false,"Intro":"The bacteria in our guts can influence the working of the mind, says Frank Swain. So could they be upgraded to enhance brainpower? as dirty, dangerous and diseased, if there is one creature that tops the list of people's most hated animal, it is the rat. But rats are clever, and usually trainable. In Tanzania, they have been taught to detect patients with tuberculosis by detecting early signs in human saliva. What would take a human scientist a whole day to diagnose the disease takes a rat a mere seven minutes.&lt;/p&gt;&lt;p&gt;BBC Earth&rsquo;s Extraordinary Animals series visited the social enterprise Apopo in Tanzania to see how the rats are trained, and discover what makes a rat's sense of smell so superior. A rat makes about eight sniffs a second compared with two for humans. Rats also smell in stereo, distinguishing two similar odours with one sniff. And around one in every 100 of a rat&rsquo;s genes is involved with odour detection, compared with one in 1,000 humans.&lt;/p&gt;&lt;p&gt;&lt;em&gt;For more videos subscribe to the &lt;/em&gt;&lt;a href=\"https://www.youtube.com/channel/UCwmZiChSryoWQCZMIQezgTg\"&gt;BBC Earth channel on YouTube&lt;/a&gt;&lt;em&gt;.&lt;/em&gt;&lt;/p&gt;&lt;p&gt;&lt;em&gt;If you would like to comment on this video or anything else you have seen on Future, head over to our &lt;/em&gt;&lt;a href=\"https://www.facebook.com/BBCFuture\"&gt;&lt;em&gt;Facebook&lt;/em&gt;&lt;/a&gt;&lt;em&gt; Environment","Name":"Science &amp; Environment","Partner":null,"PrimaryVertical":"wwfuture","Title":"Science &amp; Environment"},"Metadata":{"CreationDateTime":"2015-05-20T10:51:45.984509Z","Entity":"collection","Guid":"1aa4ccec-87b5-44b8-b5af-c553debaf6f6","Id":"wwfuture/section/science-environment","ModifiedDateTime":"2015-05-20T13:51:20.083392Z","Project":"wwfuture","Slug":"section/science-environment"},"Urn":"urn:pubstack:jative:collection:wwfuture/section/science-environment","_id":"559a52620b808738c4e262dc"}],"DisableAdverts":false,"DisplayDate":"2014-05-23T00:00:00Z","HeadlineLong":"Giant rats that can sniff out TB","HeadlineShort":"Giant rats that can sniff out TB","HideRelated":false,"Intro":"Scientists have trained the much-maligned rodent to detect tuberculosis much quicker than humans. What is it that makes a rat's sense of smell so much better than ours?","IsSyndicated":false,"Location":null,"Partner":null,"PrimaryVertical":"wwfuture","Programme":null,"RelatedStory":[{"Content":{"AssetCustom":"","AssetIbroadcast":null,"AssetImage":[],"AssetImagePromo":null,"AssetInfographic":"","AssetInline":[],"AssetSelect":"","AssetVideoIb2":null,"AssetVideoMps":null,"Author":[],"BodyHtml":"&lt;p&gt;Although she didn&rsquo;t let on that anything was wrong, by the time the leaves began to change Suci&rsquo;s keepers knew she was in trouble. The Sumatran rhino had dropped too much weight over the summer. By December, she had stopped playing with her toys and became even more reclusive and lethargic.&lt;/p&gt;&lt;p&gt;For nearly a decade, her keepers at Cincinnati Zoo had watched her grow. They had big plans for her. Suci, whose very name means &lsquo;sacred individual&rsquo; in Indonesian, was to be a saviour of her species. There are only around 100 Sumatran rhinos left, so a plan was hatched to mate Suci with her brother Harapan to increase their species&rsquo; count. But now, she had iron storage disease, a condition that also killed her mother.&lt;/p&gt;&lt;p&gt;Hope that she would pull through came to an end one Sunday in March. Her keepers noticed that she was behaving oddly that day, so stayed with her into the night, and watched as she slipped away.&lt;/p&gt;&lt;p&gt;&ldquo;I don&rsquo;t know of anyone who&rsquo;s tried as hard as we did to cure a rhino of this disease,&rdquo; says Terri Roth, director of the Center for Conservation and Research of Endangered Wildlife at the Cincinnati Zoo and Botanical Garden. But still, she says, &ldquo;You can&rsquo;t help but feel responsible that you were not able to save this animal.&rdquo; Suci, she explains, was one of the few promising females left for breeding. Others are too old or haven&rsquo;t been able to get pregnant. Even wild females are turning up infertile.&lt;/p&gt;&lt;p&gt;&ldquo;Animals die, animals get injured,&rdquo; says Roth. &ldquo;But when you&rsquo;re down to 100, it&rsquo;s far more than an emotional loss.&rdquo;&nbsp;&lt;/p&gt;&lt;p&gt;{\"image\":{\"pid\":\"p01ysfsm\"}}&lt;/p&gt;&lt;p&gt;The Sumatran rhino is one of the world&rsquo;s rarest animals, but the bittersweet news is that we can at least identify that the species is in need of our help. For other endangered animals, it can be more difficult. Protecting the world&rsquo;s rarest species from extinction first requires identifying which creatures belong in this grim category. Yet answering this simple question can prove more difficult than it might seem.&lt;/p&gt;&lt;p&gt;First off, there is a difference between population numbers and conservation status, as reported by something like the &lt;a href=\"http://www.iucnredlist.org/\"&gt;International Union for Conservation of Nature&rsquo;s (IUCN) Red List&lt;/a&gt; &ndash; the world&rsquo;s most comprehensive database on the subject. In theory, conservation statuses are based on pure science, but in reality human subjectivity can come into play. &ldquo;The listings can be incredibly political, and very emotional,&rdquo; says Lisa Campbell, the Rachel Carson associate professor of marine affairs and policy at Duke University, in Durham, North Carolina. Deciding whether to list an animal as critically endangered or just vulnerable could make or break funding for that creature, so there is a tendency to lean toward the direst possible listing.&lt;/p&gt;&lt;p&gt;{\"image\":{\"pid\":\"p01yslkr\"}}&lt;/p&gt;&lt;p&gt;Additionally, simply listing a species as critically endangered doesn&rsquo;t mean its rarity or chance of extinction is equal to all other creatures that share that status. Existing protection measures, types of threats, dispersal and genetic standings can all influence a species&rsquo; likelihood of actually kicking the extinction bucket. Numbers can vary widely, too. Critically endangered hawksbill turtles currently number in the thousands, whereas fewer than 100 critically endangered Seychelles sheath-tailed bats still exist in the world. &ldquo;One criticism is &lsquo;how can those be equivalent things?&rsquo; There&rsquo;s no good answer to that,&rdquo; Campbell says.&lt;/p&gt;&lt;p&gt;So while no one is arguing that endangered species lists are bad things, if you want to identify the rarest animals on earth, it&rsquo;s better to go by absolute numbers alone.&lt;/p&gt;&lt;p&gt;{\"image\":{\"pid\":\"p01yshny\"}}&lt;/p&gt;&lt;p&gt;In some cases, counting the total number of animals in a population can be easy. Researchers know every single remaining black stilt in New Zealand, for example. Those 27 birds are tagged and recognised like well-known friends. In other cases, it is straightforward to count the animals, such as by flying overhead to tally up African elephants. Often, though, they are stuck with less reliable methods of estimating population, such as analysing scat, counting nests or setting up camera traps. &ldquo;There&rsquo;s a whole bunch of species where we know there&rsquo;s five, 20 or 100 individuals,&rdquo; says Mike Hoffman, senior scientific officer at the IUCN&rsquo;s species survival commission. &ldquo;But there&rsquo;s a whole bunch of others that we know are highly threatened and declining quickly, but we don&rsquo;t actually know what their exact population size is.&rdquo;&lt;/p&gt;&lt;p&gt;&ldquo;So you can come up with a list of species that are perilously close to extinction,&rdquo; he says, &ldquo;but it wouldn&rsquo;t necessarily be the complete list.&rdquo;&lt;/p&gt;&lt;p&gt;Some of the species that we do know enough about to say are among the world&rsquo;s rarest include the Javan rhino, already extinct in mainland Southeast Asia and currently holding out with as few as 40 individuals; the Hainan gibbon, confined to a tiny patch of forest on China&rsquo;s rapidly developing Hainan island and numbering 23-25 individuals; and the Yangtze giant soft shell turtle, down to only four living individuals.&lt;/p&gt;&lt;p&gt;{\"image\":{\"pid\":\"p01ysggq\"}}&lt;/p&gt;&lt;p&gt;Very rare species may also persist only in captivity. The Red List currently reports 32 such species. Those species &ndash; including the Kihansi spray toad, the Socorro dove, the Guam rail and the Hawaiian crow &ndash; are strong contenders for title of rarest animal.&lt;/p&gt;&lt;p&gt;Sometimes, attempts to return these animals to the wild prove successful, as in the case of the black-footed ferret, the California condor, the Przewalski&rsquo;s horse and several others. While still listed as endangered, those animals are at least playing functional roles in their environments. In other cases, however, things don&rsquo;t go as well.&lt;/p&gt;&lt;p&gt;{\"image\":{\"pid\":\"p01ysgbh\"}}&lt;/p&gt;&lt;p&gt;Despite best efforts, the species might not breed or survive well in captivity (as in the case of Suci), or there may just be too few individuals left. Such was the case for the Po&rsquo;o-uli, a handsome Hawaiian bird whose last known individual &ndash; a lone male for whom researchers never found a mate &ndash; died in captivity in 2004. &ldquo;That was it &ndash; the end of the species,&rdquo; Hoffman says. &ldquo;It was a case of too little, too late.&rdquo;&lt;/p&gt;&lt;p&gt;{\"image\":{\"pid\":\"p01ysg40\"}}&lt;/p&gt;&lt;p&gt;Another problem with identifying the world&rsquo;s rarest animals is the difficulty often associated with seeking out the rarest of the rare. Exceptionally scarce animals can be so elusive that scientists sometimes lose track of them altogether. This might lead to their being declared extinct, only to reemerge years later. Recently, for example, the Hula painted frog &ndash; presumed extinct &ndash; hopped out of hiding in Israel after a more than 50-year hiatus.&lt;/p&gt;&lt;p&gt;Prematurely declaring a species extinct can help push it over the edge to actual extinction. Budgets and conservation planning often revolve around a species presence or non-presence in an area. If it&rsquo;s listed as officially no longer existing, then no help will come for any lone holdouts. &ldquo;One of my colleagues here coined term &lsquo;the Romeo effect,&rsquo;&rdquo; says Stuart Butchart, head of science for BirdLife International. &ldquo;It means you give up on a species and stop trying to apply conservation measures, but it does still exist.&rdquo;&lt;/p&gt;&lt;p&gt;{\"image\":{\"pid\":\"p01ysglr\"}}&lt;/p&gt;&lt;p&gt;Because of this problem, scientists who determine conservation status listings are not quick to declare extinction, even if that might very well be the case. Miss Waldron&rsquo;s red colobus has not been spotted since 1978, for example, but it is still listed as critically endangered on the Red List, with the hope that a few of those animals might be hiding out in a remote corner of the Ivory Coast jungle. Likewise, BirdLife International estimates that one to seven Oahu alauahios still survive in Hawaii, despite none having been officially sighted since 1985. &ldquo;Some of these animals flagged as possibly extinct could very well be the world&rsquo;s rarest animals if they still exist,&rdquo; Hoffman says.&nbsp;&lt;/p&gt;&lt;p&gt;Another flaw in the list of potential rarest animals on Earth is the fact that mammals, birds and reptiles dominate it. Invertebrates, fungi and many ocean-dwelling creatures are conspicuously missing simply because their global populations and conservation statuses have never been assessed.&lt;/p&gt;&lt;p&gt;{\"image\":{\"pid\":\"p01yshz6\"}}&lt;/p&gt;&lt;p&gt;The IUCN has evaluated 71,576 species out of what some researchers estimate to be 8.7 million. The organisation plans to increase that count to 160,000 by 2020, but that&rsquo;s still just a fraction of the world&rsquo;s total biodiversity. &ldquo;Defining the rarest animal on the planet would imply surveying the entire potential distribution range of every single species, and being able to count every single individual &ndash; a formidable challenge,&rdquo; says Markus Gusset, chief conservation officer at the World Association of Zoos and Aquariums.&lt;/p&gt;&lt;p&gt;This means that the world&rsquo;s rarest creature could in fact be some ultra-obscure beetle whose jungle plot is currently being mowed down, or an unknown crustacean whose estuary home is quickly being drained. It&rsquo;s simply impossible to know for sure.&lt;/p&gt;&lt;p&gt;The good news, however, is that conservation measures to protect better-studied species often benefit those the spotlight evades as well. According to BirdLife International, important bird areas, for example, fortuitously overlap with 80% of the areas identified as critical for amphibians, fish, invertebrates, plants and other life forms. This is where a species conservation status arguably becomes most important: as a tool for encouraging countries to step up and protect an endangered species before it is too late, and in so doing to also shield an entire ecosystem. &ldquo;We have the knowledge and the tools to protect these species,&rdquo; Butchart says. &ldquo;We just need to implement them.&rdquo;&lt;/p&gt;&lt;p&gt;&lt;em&gt;If you would like to comment on this, or anything else you have seen on Future, head over to our &lt;/em&gt;&lt;a href=\"https://www.facebook.com/BBCFuture\"&gt;Facebook&lt;/a&gt;&lt;em&gt; or &lt;/em&gt;&lt;a href=\"https://plus.google.com/u/0/b/107828172298602173375/107828172298602173375/posts\"&gt;Google+&lt;/a&gt;&lt;em&gt; page, or message us on &lt;/em&gt;&lt;a href=\"https://twitter.com/BBC_Future\"&gt;Twitter&lt;/a&gt;&lt;em&gt;.&lt;/em&gt;&lt;/p&gt;","BusinessUnit":"worldwide","CalloutBody":"","CalloutPosition":"","CalloutSubtitle":"","CalloutTitle":"","Campaign":null,"Collection":[],"DisableAdverts":false,"DisplayDate":"2014-05-09T00:00:00Z","HeadlineLong":"Endangered species: The last animals of their kind","HeadlineShort":"Whats the worlds rarest animal?","HideRelated":false,"Intro":"What are the rarest creatures in the world? This is a surprisingly difficult question to answer, but there are a few likely contenders, finds Rachel Nuwer.","IsSyndicated":true,"Location":null,"Partner":null,"PrimaryVertical":"wwfuture","Programme":null,"RelatedStory":[],"RelatedTag":null,"StoryType":"image","SummaryLong":"What are the rarest creatures in the world? This is a surprisingly difficult question to answer, but there are a few likely contenders, finds Rachel Nuwer","SummaryShort":"The last creatures of their kind","Tag":[]},"Metadata":{"CreationDateTime":"2014-11-18T09:16:36Z","Entity":"story","Guid":"fbbb7db4-cd13-400c-b81e-2caf13c5091f","Id":"wwfuture/story/20140509-whats-the-worlds-rarest-animal","ModifiedDateTime":"2014-11-18T09:16:36Z","Project":"wwfuture","Slug":"20140509-whats-the-worlds-rarest-animal"},"Urn":"urn:pubstack:jative:story:wwfuture/story/20140509-whats-the-worlds-rarest-animal","_id":"559a56330b808738c4e2f7e5"},{"Content":{"AssetCustom":"","AssetIbroadcast":null,"AssetImage":[],"AssetImagePromo":null,"AssetInfographic":"","AssetInline":[],"AssetSelect":"","AssetVideoIb2":null,"AssetVideoMps":null,"Author":[],"BodyHtml":"&lt;p&gt;Imagine that your heart is failing. You desperately need a replacement. Every day, your family waits nervously for a call from the hospital to say they&rsquo;ve found a donor. Then one day, the call comes through. In your excitement you barely hear what the doctor on the other end of the line is telling you. There&rsquo;s something you should know, she says. The donor is not human. It&rsquo;s a pig.&nbsp;&lt;/p&gt;&lt;p&gt;That possibility crept a little closer last week, with the announcement that a pig&rsquo;s heart had survived over a year after it was transplanted to a baboon. The work, led by Dr Muhammad Mohuiddin at the US National Heart, Lung and Blood Institute in Bethesda, Maryland, supports the case of those who say that animal organs could be used to help humans on long transplant waiting lists.&lt;/p&gt;&lt;p&gt;Such procedures would raise all sorts of thorny issues about what it will mean to be fully human in the future. If we can transplant pig hearts, then what other parts could we use to augment our bodies? And will taking non-human organs transform our relationship with animals, and even each other?&lt;/p&gt;&lt;p&gt;{\"image\":{\"pid\":\"p01yptsv\"}}&lt;/p&gt;&lt;p&gt;Xenotransplantation &ndash; using animal tissues in humans &ndash; dates back to at least 1682, when Dutch surgeon Job Janszoon van Meekeren reported that a Russian soldier&rsquo;s skull had been repaired with a fragment of bone from a dog. Horrified church authorities ordered the removal of the graft, but it had healed too well to be removed. Later, Alexis Carrel&rsquo;s pioneering techniques for suturing blood vessels paved the way for the first xenotransplanted organs in 1902, but it took until the 1960s for any meaningful progress, when&lt;a href=\"http://news.bbc.co.uk/2/hi/science/nature/425120.stm\"&gt; surgeons had limited success transplanting primate organs to humans&lt;/a&gt;. However most failed within couple of months, and the patients died.&lt;/p&gt;&lt;p&gt;Today, however, primates are no longer considered viable donors, due to issues such as disease transmission risk and the ethical considerations of primate research. What&rsquo;s more, the body would probably reject the organs. &ldquo;The major obstruction to xenotransplantation was the immunological rejection,&rdquo; says Mohuiddin.&lt;/p&gt;&lt;p&gt;Pigs, however, have proven to be better donors, at least in tests on baboons. A pig&rsquo;s heart is anatomically similar to a human, they pose less of a disease risk and the animals grow quickly, making them an excellent substitute. Crucially, by modifying a pig&rsquo;s genetics, Mohuiddin was able to render the transplanted hearts invisible to the baboon&rsquo;s immune system. Two genetic tweaks reduced the ability of the baboon&rsquo;s immune cells to identify the heart as a foreign body. A third added a gene that produces a human anti-clotting agent to help counter immune system reactions that can be triggered by blood clots forming around foreign tissue. Together, these changes allowed the heart to survive far longer than previous attempts.&lt;strong&gt;&lt;/p&gt;&lt;p&gt;&lt;/strong&gt;{\"image\":{\"pid\":\"p01ypty3\"}}&lt;strong&gt;&lt;/p&gt;&lt;p&gt;&lt;/strong&gt;So, what would a world with animal organ transplants in humans look like? Might we one day see farmers in overalls mucking out pens where pigs roll in the hay and oink happily as they grow a stock of organs inside them? Perhaps that doesn&rsquo;t seem too different from the situation we have now. We have after all been rearing pigs for their meat for thousands of years, and transplantable pig hearts will probably sell for a good deal more than they currently fetches as offal.&lt;/p&gt;&lt;p&gt;Indeed, a ready supply of compatible organs might open up unexpected demand for animal products. Medical interventions &ndash; even very extreme ones &ndash; have a habit of normalising once the costs and risks fall. Who would have thought that life-saving blood transfusions might one day be used by athletes to gain an edge on their competitors, or that surgery to repair the faces of disfigured soldiers would find its home in the opulent clinics of Beverly Hills, shoring up the faces of wealthy clients against wrinkles and sunspots?&lt;/p&gt;&lt;p&gt;While we can only speculate, breaking down the biological barriers that separate us from the beasts could lead to all kinds of elective procedures. As I wrote last month, &lt;a href=\"http://www.bbc.com/future/story/20140407-the-people-with-animal-powers\"&gt;many people already covet &lsquo;animal powers&rsquo;, which they aim to acquire via technology&lt;/a&gt;. Eventually, we might opt for cosmetic changes, and attach the oversized canines of wild animals to our teeth like the cyberpunks of William Gibson&rsquo;s novel Neuromancer. Alternatively, an athlete looking for a boost could switch her heart for something bigger, stronger, indefatigable &ndash; and maybe switch back again once her competitive days were over. &nbsp;&lt;/p&gt;&lt;p&gt;{\"image\":{\"pid\":\"p01ypyr6\"}}&lt;/p&gt;&lt;p&gt;What is perhaps more interesting to ask is whether elevating the humble porker to life-saving superhero will change people&rsquo;s relationship with the animal they are more used to seeing on their plates. Would you hesitate to sit down to a Sunday roast, knowing the pork on the table had the same heart beating in its chest as your great uncle? I put that question to Mohuiddin, but he declined to speculate on the issue.&lt;/p&gt;&lt;p&gt;Nonetheless, genetically augmenting pigs to make them more suitable as organ donors marks a small but important nudge toward them occupying some middle ground between food and friend. By expressing some of our genes, these pigs are, after all, very slightly human, and will become incrementally more so as the genes which present incompatibility issues are identified and swapped for human-tolerant ones.&lt;/p&gt;&lt;p&gt;Perhaps one day we&rsquo;ll even go so far as to breed personalised pets that complement you perfectly, should you find yourself in sudden need of a heart, a kidney, or a liver. This ought to trouble our ethics far less than attempts to conceive a &lsquo;saviour sibling&rsquo; &ndash; a child born in order to provide life-saving tissue donations to a desperately ill brother or sister.&nbsp;&lt;/p&gt;&lt;p&gt;Breeding the flesh of an animal to replace life-giving organs will undeniably change what these creatures are to us, and what it means to be &lsquo;fully&rsquo; human. You only have to listen to your own heart beating in your chest right now to understand why.&lt;/p&gt;&lt;p&gt;&lt;em&gt;If you would like to comment on this, or anything else you have seen on Future, head over to our &lt;/em&gt;&lt;a href=\"https://www.facebook.com/BBCFuture\"&gt;&lt;em&gt;Facebook&lt;/em&gt;&lt;/a&gt;&lt;em&gt; or &lt;/em&gt;&lt;a href=\"https://plus.google.com/u/0/b/107828172298602173375/107828172298602173375/posts\"&gt;&lt;em&gt;Google+&lt;/em&gt;&lt;/a&gt;&lt;em&gt; page, or message us on &lt;/em&gt;&lt;a href=\"https://twitter.com/BBC_Future\"&gt;&lt;em&gt;Twitter&lt;/em&gt;&lt;/a&gt;&lt;/p&gt;","BusinessUnit":"worldwide","CalloutBody":"","CalloutPosition":"","CalloutSubtitle":"","CalloutTitle":"","Campaign":null,"Collection":[],"DisableAdverts":false,"DisplayDate":"2014-05-08T00:00:00Z","HeadlineLong":"Would you accept a pigs heart?","HeadlineShort":"Could pig transplants save lives?","HideRelated":false,"Intro":"If animal organs could be used to save our lives, will it mean we become a bit less human? (AlamyCelebrity/Alamy)","TemplateUrl":"http://ichef.bbci.co.uk/wwfeatures/$recipe/images/live/p0/1w/zs/p01wzs1b.jpg","Title":"nadya_vessey_fin_crop.jpg"},"Metadata":{"CreationDateTime":"0001-01-01T00:00:00Z","Entity":"","Guid":"","Id":"p01wzs1b","ModifiedDateTime":"0001-01-01T00:00:00Z","Project":"","Slug":""},"Urn":"urn:pubstack:jative:image:p01wzs1b","_id":"559a54970b808738c4e2bcf0"},{"Content":{"FileSizeBytes":50847,"MimeType":"image/jpeg","SourceHeight":549,"SourceUrl":"http://deltaorigin.bbc.co.uk/images/live/p0/1w/zs/p01wzsmn.jpg","SourceWidth":976,"SynopsisLong":"Bats navigate by echolocation; some blind people can too, by clicking their mouth and listening to the echo (SPL)","SynopsisMedium":"Bats navigate by echolocation; some blind people can too, by clicking their mouth and listening to the echo (SPL)","SynopsisShort":"Bat echolocation (SPL)","TemplateUrl":"http://ichef.bbci.co.uk/wwfeatures/$recipe/images/live/p0/1w/zs/p01wzsmn.jpg","Title":"bat_crop.jpg"},"Metadata":{"CreationDateTime":"0001-01-01T00:00:00Z","Entity":"","Guid":"","Id":"p01wzsmn","ModifiedDateTime":"0001-01-01T00:00:00Z","Project":"","Slug":""},"Urn":"urn:pubstack:jative:image:p01wzsmn","_id":"559a54970b808738c4e2bcf1"}],"AssetSelect":"","AssetVideoIb2":null,"AssetVideoMps":null,"Author":[{"Content":{"AssetImage":[],"Description":"&lt;p&gt;&lt;a href=\"http://www.frankswain.com/\"&gt;Frank Swain&lt;/a&gt; is Communities Editor at New Scientist,\nauthor of How To Make A Zombie, and a freelance science writer for\nMosaic, Wired, Slate, BBC Radio 4, and others. You can find him on Twitter as\n&lt;a href=\"https://twitter.com/SciencePunk\"&gt;@SciencePunk&lt;/a&gt;.&lt;/p&gt;","Email":[],"Links":null,"Name":"Frank Swain","PrimaryVertical":"wwfuture"},"Metadata":{"CreationDateTime":"2014-11-18T09:15:45Z","Entity":"author","Guid":"dadbb9fc-1bad-4a87-851c-cb268f3505a8","Id":"wwfuture/author/frank-swain","ModifiedDateTime":"2014-11-18T09:15:45Z","Project":"wwfuture","Slug":"frank-swain"},"Urn":"urn:pubstack:jative:author:wwfuture/author/frank-swain","_id":"559a524c0b808738c4e25f66"}],"BodyHtml":"&lt;p&gt;At the end of World War I, the aristocratic Horace Ridler returned to Britain from Mesopotamia a changed man. Turning his back on his privileged upbringing, Ridler tattooed his face and body in swirling patterns, took to wearing a large nose ring and stretched out his earlobes. Donning a costume of jewelled robes, he reinvented himself as Omi the Zebra Man and toured with circuses such as Barnum and Bailey, and Ripley&rsquo;s Odditorium.&lt;/p&gt;&lt;p&gt;Ridler was just one in a long line of people who devoted themselves to taking on animal-like appeareances. The American Dennis Avner, better known as Stalking Cat or simply Cat Man, underwent over 14 surgical procedures to transform his face into a feline appearance, sculpting his cheeks, lips and eyes, and adding striped tattoos, whiskers and a set of needle-like teeth.&lt;/p&gt;&lt;p&gt;And for 20 years, the Leopard Man of Skye could be glimpsed living in a derelict stone cottage in the far north of Scotland, recognisable for his head-to-toe tattooed spots. Tom Leppard, as he is known to his friends, moved five years ago into a more comfortable home on the mainland at 73 years of age &ndash; not bad for a cat.&lt;/p&gt;&lt;p&gt;{\"image\":{\"pid\":\"p01wzrrb\"}}&lt;/p&gt;&lt;p&gt;Few people would willingly transform their looks in such a way, and so these people gained celebrity status in their lifetime. Now, some prosthetics wearers, body hackers and amateur scientists are taking this to a different level&lt;strong&gt;. &lt;/strong&gt;With technology and biology at their disposal they are aiming to mimic animal abilities &ndash; not their appearance &ndash; and they can already do things once thought impossible for humans.&lt;br /&gt; &lt;strong&gt;&lt;br /&gt; &lt;/strong&gt;Some have been augmenting their physical abilities in places we haven&rsquo;t evolved to thrive, such as water. Nadya Vessey, for example, was born with a condition that prevented her legs developing properly. A few years ago, she had a mermaid tail designed that allows her to glide through the water like a dolphin.&lt;/p&gt;&lt;p&gt;Others are turning to technology to sense a world that lies hidden to everybody else. Take, for instance, the invisible world of electromagnetic fields. Sharks have pits in their snout called the ampullae of Lorenzini with which they can sense disturbances in the electric field caused by their prey. Birds, lobsters and bees, meanwhile, are thought to sense magnetic fields, which some use to navigate.&lt;/p&gt;&lt;p&gt;&lt;strong&gt;Sixth sense&lt;/strong&gt;&lt;/p&gt;&lt;p&gt;In recent years, body modification artists have been experimenting with magnetic implants to achieve a similar &ldquo;sixth sense&rdquo;. By implanting tiny disc-shaped magnets in their fingers, they can feel the electromagnetic fields surrounding everyday technology. The falling price and wider availability of powerful neodymium magnets have made the procedure increasingly popular.&lt;/p&gt;&lt;p&gt;Peyton Rowlands is one such enthusiast, a Texas-based biohacker who implanted a magnet into the ring finger of his left hand. &ldquo;You don&rsquo;t feel your finger getting pulled toward anything because the magnet is just so small,&rdquo; he explains. &ldquo;You feel a slight buzz once you get within a couple of inches of basically anything with electricity running through it.&rdquo; With the implant, Rowlands found he could detect the fields surrounding microwaves, refrigerators and power converters. &ldquo;It&rsquo;s a very interesting sensation, kind of crazy. One of the things that I noticed is that DC current often felt similar to a ferrous metal or another magnet, a static bubble pushing or pulling against you, as opposed to AC currents which were much more like the kind of pins and needles feeling people describe.&rdquo;&lt;/p&gt;&lt;p&gt;{\"image\":{\"pid\":\"p01wzs1b\"}}&lt;/p&gt;&lt;p&gt;However, Rowlands suffered a reaction to the magnet and had to remove it. This is a relatively common risk with implants, and they need to be coated with some kind of biocompatible material to avoid being expelled by the body. However, even these coatings can cause a reaction, as Rowlands discovered. He&rsquo;s currently searching for a more tissue-friendly alternative.&lt;/p&gt;&lt;p&gt;Gaining an animal ability does not have to mean relying on technology. Daniel Kish lost his sight at the age of 13 months after suffering retinal blastoma, a form of cancer that affects the eyes. Within two months, his parents noticed he seemed to be able to navigate his environment and had a sense of what was around him. &ldquo;Echolocation is something I&rsquo;ve done for as long as I can remember,&rdquo; he says. &ldquo;The clicking of echolocation is used discreetly and strategically, so it isn&rsquo;t something that would necessarily be immediately obvious to my parents.&rdquo;&lt;/p&gt;&lt;p&gt;&lt;strong&gt;Bat man&lt;/strong&gt;&lt;/p&gt;&lt;p&gt;Quite on his own, Kish had learned to visualise the environment by clicking his tongue and listening to the reflected sounds. Echolocation is used by bats as well as cetaceans such as dolphins and whales to sense their environment. Kish identifies at least two kinds of echolocation he uses &ndash; the active clicking which he calls &ldquo;flash sonar&rdquo; (in reference to the illuminating flash of a camera), and passive echolocation which relies on paying careful attention to the acoustics of the immediate environment. Together, they form a small subset of the skills he uses to compensate for his lack of sight.&lt;/p&gt;&lt;p&gt;&ldquo;There&rsquo;s a misconception out there that you have to be gifted to develop this skill,&rdquo; he says. &ldquo;I would say that&rsquo;s not true. You get ranges of ability, but it really has most to do with the motivation one has and the opportunity to implement the skill.&rdquo;&lt;/p&gt;&lt;p&gt;Indeed, others are already following Kish, such as Sam Oldridge, a British boy who was echolocating by the time he was eight years old (see video, below). And as president of World Access for the Blind, Kish runs workshops to teach blind students how to echolocate as well receive proper cane training.&lt;/p&gt;&lt;p&gt;{\"magnify\":{ \"id\": \"Blind-Boy-Sees-With-Sound-Echol\"}}&lt;/p&gt;&lt;p&gt;&ldquo;The brain is a very interesting mechanism, a massive adaptive mechanism,&rdquo; says Kish. But admits it&rsquo;s very difficult for a sighted person to learn echolocation, because they lack the motivating necessity. &ldquo;Learning echolocation is much easier than learning a language, I know as I&rsquo;ve done both,&rdquo; Kish laughs. &ldquo;And like a language if you don&rsquo;t use it daily, you lose it.&rdquo;&lt;/p&gt;&lt;p&gt;But could sighted people train their eyes to see further, better, clearer? Rowlands thinks so. Working as a researcher with Science for the Masses, a group that works on open-access scientific research outside of any academic institution, he is embarking on a self-experiment programme to augment his own eyes to see into the infra-red spectrum through diet alone.&lt;/p&gt;&lt;p&gt;The key is in the biology of the eye. The human retina predominately uses the molecules photopsin and rhodopsin to perceive light, while freshwater fish such as the Mozambique tilapia employ a different molecule, porphyropsin. This grants the tilapia a much greater sensitivity to deep reds, and allows them to see into the near infra-red (NIR) spectrum. Rowlands hopes that by avoiding retinol (Vitamin A) &ndash; the vital nutrient needed to produce photopsin and rhodopsin, he can force the body into using its less-preferred molecule, porphyropsin.&lt;/p&gt;&lt;p&gt;As crazy as it sounds, the experiment is not without precedent. During World War II, the US Navy tried a similar procedure to give its signalmen NIR vision. The goal was to allow ships to communicate via infra-red semaphore lamps, which the enemy would not be able to see. However, the advent of infra-red goggles killed the idea.&lt;/p&gt;&lt;p&gt;{\"image\":{\"pid\":\"p01wzsmn\"}}&lt;/p&gt;&lt;p&gt;&ldquo;With this exploit, I&rsquo;m not seeing in the thermal range,&rdquo; warns Rowlands. &ldquo;I don&rsquo;t want to give the impression we&rsquo;re talking about &lsquo;Predator vision&rsquo;&rdquo;. However, if successful, the augment will allow him to see more clearly, as NIR light has an easier time passing through smog and dust. &ldquo;I would have a longer range of vision, potentially I would see a lot further out on the horizon than anyone else,&rdquo; he explains. &ldquo;The same thing goes for sunglasses and tinted car windows, I&rsquo;ll be able to see through these things a lot easier.&rdquo; Rowlands says he might also find he can see the lights emitted by security night cameras and TV remotes, and even see through certain clothing materials.&lt;/p&gt;&lt;p&gt;Clearly the plan comes with risks. Withholding essential nutrients from the body can be harmful, which is why Rowlands and the other experimenters have designed a specially-designed nutrient shake containing supplements designed to counteract some of the risks. The fact that Rowlands&rsquo; group is working outside university science, without any of the usual qualifications, may raise some eyebrows. But he&rsquo;s determined to prove that science can exist outside of academia, by following the scientific method and using established experimental protocols. They&rsquo;ll find out if it works after a three-month-long trial, beginning soon.&nbsp;&nbsp; &nbsp;&lt;/p&gt;&lt;p&gt;It&rsquo;s easy to look at the animal kingdom and feel in awe of the incredible feats on display. But both Rowlands and Kish warn that humans shouldn&rsquo;t be underestimated. &ldquo;We can&rsquo;t pre-suppose restrictions on any individual,&rdquo; says Kish. &ldquo;Judging by the ease with which I can train someone to use a cane and flash sonar suggests that the ability is [already] there, it just needs to be activated. It&rsquo;s quite rusty, but it&rsquo;s there.&rdquo; There&rsquo;s no doubt that our brains provide us with imagination and innovation that no other creature can match &ndash; replicating unusual animal abilities might just be another of our resulting talents. How's that for a superpower?&lt;/p&gt;&lt;p&gt;&lt;em&gt;If you would like to comment on this, or anything else you have seen on Future, head over to our &lt;/em&gt;&lt;a href=\"https://www.facebook.com/BBCFuture\"&gt;&lt;em&gt;Facebook&lt;/em&gt;&lt;/a&gt;&lt;em&gt; or &lt;/em&gt;&lt;a href=\"https://plus.google.com/u/0/b/107828172298602173375/107828172298602173375/posts\"&gt;&lt;em&gt;Google+&lt;/em&gt;&lt;/a&gt;&lt;em&gt; page, or message us on &lt;/em&gt;&lt;a href=\"https://twitter.com/BBC_Future\"&gt;&lt;em&gt;Twitter&lt;/em&gt;&lt;/a&gt;&lt;em&gt;.&lt;/em&gt;&lt;/p&gt;","BusinessUnit":"worldwide","CalloutBody":"","CalloutPosition":"","CalloutSubtitle":"","CalloutTitle":"","Campaign":null,"Collection":[{"Content":{"AssetImage":[],"Campaign":null,"CollectionOverrides":null,"CollectionType":"column","Description":"How technology is enhancing the body and mind. Writer Frank Swain explores the social, legal, and ethical impact of devices and prosthetics that promise to augment our natural abilities, senses and brainpower.","Name":"Beyond Human","Partner":null,"PrimaryVertical":"wwfuture","Title":"Beyond Human","CreationDateTime":"2015-05-20T11:23:36.04838Z","Entity":"collection","Guid":"ae993461-edb9-4ed7-8398-6098043049c9","Id":"wwfuture/column/beyond-human","ModifiedDateTime":"2015-05-20T11:23:36.04838Z","Project":"wwfuture","Slug":"column/beyond-human"},"Metadata":{"CreationDateTime":"2015-05-20T11:23:36.04838Z","Entity":"collection","Guid":"ae993461-edb9-4ed7-8398-6098043049c9","Id":"wwfuture/column/beyond-human","ModifiedDateTime":"2015-05-20T11:23:36.04838Z","Project":"wwfuture","Slug":"column/beyond-human"},"Urn":"urn:pubstack:jative:collection:wwfuture/column/beyond-human","_id":"559a52610b808738c4e26298"},{"Content":{"AssetImage":[],"Campaign":null,"CollectionOverrides":[],"CollectionType":"section","Description":"Technology","Name":"Technology","Partner":null,"PrimaryVertical":"wwfuture","Title":"Technology"},"Metadata":{"CreationDateTime":"2015-05-20T11:42:22.339015Z","Entity":"collection","Guid":"fadfa439-a201-46b6-a8fd-d8813cac4bbf","Id":"wwfuture/section/technology","ModifiedDateTime":"2015-05-20T13:49:02.740858Z","Project":"wwfuture","Slug":"section/technology"},"Urn":"urn:pubstack:jative:collection:wwfuture/section/technology","_id":"559a52620b808738c4e262dd"},{"Content":{"AssetImage":[],"Campaign":null,"CollectionOverrides":[],"CollectionType":"section","Description":"Health","Name":"Health","Partner":null,"PrimaryVertical":"wwfuture","Title":"Health"},"Metadata":{"CreationDateTime":"2015-05-20T10:44:06.503434Z","Entity":"collection","Guid":"00a9a33a-e393-4d09-8029-7e9d5bd1d8cb","Id":"wwfuture/section/health","ModifiedDateTime":"2015-05-20T13:52:37.074442Z","Project":"wwfuture","Slug":"section/health"},"Urn":"urn:pubstack:jative:collection:wwfuture/section/health","_id":"559a52620b808738c4e262db"},{"Content":{"AssetImage":[],"Campaign":null,"CollectionOverrides":[],"CollectionType":"section","Description":"Science &amp; Environment","Name":"Science &amp; Environment","Partner":null,"PrimaryVertical":"wwfuture","Title":"Science &amp; Environment"},"Metadata":{"CreationDateTime":"2015-05-20T10:51:45.984509Z","Entity":"collection","Guid":"1aa4ccec-87b5-44b8-b5af-c553debaf6f6","Id":"wwfuture/section/science-environment","ModifiedDateTime":"2015-05-20T13:51:20.083392Z","Project":"wwfuture","Slug":"section/science-environment"},"Urn":"urn:pubstack:jative:collection:wwfuture/section/science-environment","_id":"559a52620b808738c4e262dc"}],"DisableAdverts":false,"DisplayDate":"2014-11-18T09:14:40Z","HeadlineLong":"The people with animal powers","HeadlineShort":"The people with animal powers","HideRelated":false,"Intro":"By augmenting their bodies and minds to acquire animal-like abilities, some people are trying to sense a world that most humans cannot experience, says Frank Swain.","IsSyndicated":true,"Location":null,"Partner":null,"PrimaryVertical":"wwfuture","Programme":null,"RelatedStory":[{"Content":{"AssetCustom":"","AssetIbroadcast":null,"AssetImage":[],"AssetImagePromo":null,"AssetInfographic":"","AssetInline":[],"AssetSelect":"","AssetVideoIb2":null,"AssetVideoMps":null,"Author":[],"BodyHtml":"&lt;p&gt;This month, news outlets worldwide issued breathless reports of a wondrous implant that causes orgasms at the touch of a button. The Orgasmatron, patented by Dr Stuart Meloy, is a small box wired to the spine that can send out waves of pleasure signals whenever the user desires. Dig a little deeper though, and it turns out this technology has a strange and fascinating backstory.&lt;/p&gt;&lt;p&gt;&ldquo;You&rsquo;re about the sixth or seventh reporter to call, and I&rsquo;m wondering what is going on,&rdquo; a perplexed Meloy told me. His confusion is justifiable. Recent news reports about the device are based exclusively on &lt;a href=\"http://www.newscientist.com/article/mg16922773.800-push-my-button.html#.UyxvB84YsyY\"&gt;a 13-year-old story in New Scientist magazine&lt;/a&gt; which recently appeared on web powerhouse Reddit, a user-curated repository of interesting things. In the long interim, Meloy has been trying to attract interest and funding for his device, without success.&lt;/p&gt;&lt;p&gt;Meloy is a physician and co-founder of &lt;a href=\"http://aipainmanagement.com/\"&gt;Advanced Interventional Pain Management&lt;/a&gt;, a clinic that treats patients suffering from chronic pain. Through this centre, he began working with electronic implants. Attached to nerves in the spine, these devices send out continuous, low pulses to dull chronic pain. But following an operation to install an implant, one patient reported an unusual but not undesirable side effect: the device emitted intensely pleasurable sensations. Meloy realised that he had a powerful technology in his hands, one that he thought might be used to treat men and women suffering sexual dysfunction.&lt;/p&gt;&lt;p&gt;{\"image\":{\"pid\":\"p01w12h5\"}}&lt;/p&gt;&lt;p&gt;That was over a decade ago, and while Meloy has enjoyed a successful career as a physician, progress on the Orgasmatron has stalled. One stumbling block is the generators used, which cost around $25,000. Meloy is confident that an Orgasmatron could get by on a much smaller power source, sufficient for about an hour&rsquo;s use per day. &ldquo;Pulsing constantly for days at a time is not, in my humble opinion, all that necessary to treat sexual dysfunction,&rdquo; he says. &ldquo;Some of us have to go to work.&rdquo; Unfortunately, no suitable alternative exists, and he hasn&rsquo;t been able to convince any medical manufacturers to design one.&lt;/p&gt;&lt;p&gt;Then there is the issue of who pays for such an implant. &ldquo;Insurance companies will not pay for anything considered experimental or investigational,&rdquo; he explains. Although Meloy has fitted hundreds of patients with the devices for pain management (some of whom reported experiencing its famously positive side effect), implanting it specifically to treat sexual dysfunction would be a breach of regulations. Despite the headlines, the device still has not been shown to be an effective treatment for sexual dysfunction, and anyone thinking of faking a painful condition in order to get one risks disappointment. To get approval from the Food and Drug Administration, Meloy would have to carry out a &ldquo;pivotal trial&rdquo;, which would cost around $6m. &ldquo;That&rsquo;s money I don&rsquo;t have right now,&rdquo; he sighs.&lt;/p&gt;&lt;p&gt;&lt;strong&gt;Pleasure centre&lt;/strong&gt;&lt;/p&gt;&lt;p&gt;Strangely, Meloy isn&rsquo;t the first person to stumble upon the idea of installing a pleasure button in humans. In the 1950s, another US physician, named Robert Gabriel Heath, was treating psychological disorders at the Department of Psychiatry and Neurology at Tulane University in New Orleans. Heath wanted to develop something that was as effective as a lobotomy &ndash; still relatively common in that day &ndash; but was far less destructive. He achieved this with electrotherapy, using dentistry drills to cut tiny holes in the skulls his patients, through which thin metal probes were pushed, so that pulses of electricity could be administered directly to the brain.&lt;/p&gt;&lt;p&gt;Heath discovered that by activating the septal region, he could induce a rush of pleasure that subdued violent behaviours in by some of his patients. And when given their own pleasure switch, patients were able to manage their mood swings.&lt;/p&gt;&lt;p&gt;One patient clocked up 1,500 doses in a three-hour period, but overall, they showed surprising restraint. (Unlike rats that underwent the same procedure, which self-administered to the point of exhaustion).&lt;/p&gt;&lt;p&gt;Reportedly, Heath&rsquo;s pleasure button earned him a visit from the CIA, who wanted to know if the technology could be used to inflict pain instead, to interrogate enemies of the state &ndash; or even control their minds. Heath threw the man out of his lab. &ldquo;If I wanted to be a spy, I&rsquo;d be a spy,&rdquo; he thundered to the New York Times in an interview. &ldquo;I wanted to be a doctor and practise medicine&rdquo;.&lt;/p&gt;&lt;p&gt;Some of Heath&rsquo;s contemporaries, however, saw the wider implications of bringing human emotions to heel. &lt;a href=\"http://www.scientificamerican.com/article/the-forgotten-era-of-brai/\"&gt;Jose Manuel Rodriguez Delgado was another researcher who chanced upon the ability to manipulate pleasurable sensations in patient&rsquo;s brains&lt;/a&gt;. He also paired electronic brain stimulators with radio transceivers, effectively putting the subject under remote control. Famously, Delgado was so confident in his tech that he leapt into a bullring opposite one of his experimental animals. As the bull charged at him, Delgado was able to make it stop, bellow and turn it in circles with a flick of his remote (see video, below).&lt;/p&gt;&lt;p&gt;{\"magnify\":{ \"id\": \"Jos-Delgado-implants-and-electr\"}}&lt;/p&gt;&lt;p&gt;However, the public mood surrounding brain implants soured with the publication of his book Physical Control of the Mind: Toward a Psychocivilized Society in 1969, in which Delgado (somewhat naively) downplayed the Orwellian prospects of the devices and encouraged people to embrace the technology. If everyone would consent to implantation to mediate their tempers and traumas, the world would be a better place, he claimed. Two researchers he had briefly worked with raised an outcry the following year when they suggested the devices could be used to quell black citizens rioting in America&rsquo;s inner cities. Funding dried up, and with the advent of effective drugs to treat mental illnesses, electrical brain stimulation fell into obscurity &ndash; and with it, the joy boxes.&lt;/p&gt;&lt;p&gt;Although Meloy is enthusiastic about the potential benefits of his devices, using them as a means of social control is &ldquo;not something I subscribe to&rdquo;. He is hopeful though that the renewed interest in the Orgasmatron might give it a second chance at becoming a reality.&lt;/p&gt;&lt;p&gt;If that were to happen, can we expect to see auxiliary pleasure buttons popping up on people&rsquo;s bodies? Not so fast, says Dr Petra Boynton, a sex researcher at University College London. &ldquo;I've yet to see a device, medication or product that provides significantly better outcomes than placebo for sexual problems,&rdquo; she says. &ldquo;I&rsquo;m concerned with the idea of offering a surgical intervention for cases that most probably would've done better with therapy, or information about sexual problems, options for pleasure, and how our bodies work.&rdquo;&lt;/p&gt;&lt;p&gt;So if the Orgasmatron does ever reach the market, consider that you already have a much more powerful electric joy box sitting on your shoulders. As for those who do decide to go the technological route, just make sure you know who is pushing your buttons.&lt;/p&gt;&lt;p&gt;&lt;em&gt;If you would like to comment on this, or anything else you have seen on Future, head over to our &lt;/em&gt;&lt;a href=\"https://www.facebook.com/BBCFuture\"&gt;&lt;em&gt;Facebook&lt;/em&gt;&lt;/a&gt;&lt;em&gt;
