At least, by some counts. While you are indeed made up of billions of human cells working in remarkable concert, these are easily outnumbered by &lt;a href=\"http://www.bbc.com/future/story/20120412-the-beasts-inside-you\"&gt;the bacterial cells that live on and in you&lt;/a&gt; &ndash; your microbiome. There are ten of them for every one of your own cells, and they add an extra two kilograms (4.4lbs) to your body.&nbsp;&lt;/p&gt;&lt;p&gt;Far from being freeloading passengers, many of these microbes actively help digest food and prevent infection. And now evidence is emerging that these tiny organisms may also have a profound impact on the brain too. They are a living augmentation of your body &ndash; and like any enhancement, this means they could, in principle, be upgraded. So, could you hack your microbiome to make yourself healthier, happier, and smarter too?&lt;/p&gt;&lt;p&gt;According to John Cryan, this isn&rsquo;t as far-fetched as it sounds. As a professor of anatomy and neuroscience at University College Cork, he specialises in the relationship between the brain and the gut. One of his early experiments showed the diversity of bacteria living in the gut was greatly diminished in mice suffering from early life stress. This finding inspired him to investigate the connection between the microbiome and the brain.&lt;/p&gt;&lt;p&gt;The bacterial microbiota in the gut helps normal brain development, says Cryan. &ldquo;If you don&rsquo;t have microbiota you have major changes in brain structure and function, and then also in behaviour.&rdquo; In a pioneering study, a Japanese research team showed that mice raised without any gut bacteria had an exaggerated physical response to stress, releasing more hormone than mice that had a full complement of bacteria. However, this effect could be reduced in bacteria-free mice by repopulating their gut with &lt;em&gt;Bifidobacterium infantis&lt;/em&gt;, one of the major symbiotic bacteria found in the gut. Cryan&rsquo;s team built on this finding, showing that this effect could be reproduced even in healthy mice. &ldquo;We took healthy mice and fed them &lt;em&gt;Lactobacillus&lt;/em&gt; [another common gut bacteria), and we showed that these animals had a reduced stress response and reduced anxiety-related behaviours.&rdquo;&lt;/p&gt;&lt;p&gt;{\"image\":{\"pid\":\"p01slt3z\"}}&lt;/p&gt;&lt;p&gt;But why should bacteria in the gut affect the brain? There are several different ways that messages can be sent from one organ to the other. It can be hormones or immune cells via the bloodstream, or by impulses along the vagus nerve, which stretches from the brain to intertwine closely with the gut. Through these pathways, actions in one produce effects in the other.&lt;/p&gt;&lt;p&gt;So how might you go about altering your microbiome to do a spot of brain-hacking? Cryan&rsquo;s team works on several fronts, investigating the potential to manage stress, pain, obesity and cognition through the gut. &ldquo;We have unpublished data showing that probiotics can enhance learning in animal models,&rdquo; he tells me. His team tested the effects of two strains of bacteria, finding that one improved cognition in mice. His team is now embarking on human trials, to see if healthy volunteers can have their cognitive abilities enhanced or modulated by tweaking the gut microbiome.&lt;/p&gt;&lt;p&gt;Another method of adjusting the bacterial profile of your gut is to undergo a transplant that involves taking faecal material from a donor&rsquo;s intestine &ndash; often a close relative &ndash; and implanting into a recipient via enema infusion. This unorthodox treatment has been shown to successfully treat infections caused by pathogenic bacteria colonising the gut.&lt;/p&gt;&lt;p&gt;&lt;strong&gt;Brain boost&lt;/strong&gt;&lt;/p&gt;&lt;p&gt;Thankfully, Cryan has a far more appetising method on offer.&nbsp; &ldquo;Diet is perhaps the biggest factor in shaping the composition of the microbiome,&rdquo; he says. A study by University College Cork researchers &lt;a href=\"http://www.nature.com/nature/journal/v488/n7410/full/nature11319.html?WT.ec_id=NATURE-20120809\"&gt;published in Nature in 2012&lt;/a&gt; followed 200 elderly people over the course of two years, as they transitioned into different environments such as nursing homes. The researchers found that their subjects&rsquo; health &ndash; frailty, cognition, and immune system &ndash; all correlated with their microbiome. From bacterial population alone, researchers could tell if a patient was a long-stay patient in a nursing home, or short-stay, or living in the general community. These changes were a direct reflection of their diet in these different environments. &ldquo;A diverse diet gives you a diverse microbiome that gives you a better health outcome,&rdquo; says Cryan.&lt;/p&gt;&lt;p&gt;Beyond a healthy and varied diet, though, it still remains to be discovered whether certain food combinations could alter the microbiome to produce a cognitive boost. In fact, Cryan recommends that claims from probiotic supplements of brain-boosting ought to be taken with a pinch of salt for now. &ldquo;Unless the studies have been done, one can assume they&rsquo;re not going to have any effect on mental health,&rdquo; he says. Still, he&rsquo;s optimistic about the future. &ldquo;The field right now is evolving very strongly and quickly. There&rsquo;s a lot of important research to be done. It&rsquo;s still early days.&rdquo;&lt;/p&gt;&lt;p&gt;Hacking the brain often conjures up ideas of electrical hardware such as implants and trans-cranial stimulators. But it might be the case that a simple change in diet can shift your brain up a gear. The transhumanists and body hackers who believe that technology is the sole way to improve human ability would do well to pay as much attention to the living augmentation that already resides in their gut.&lt;/p&gt;&lt;p&gt;&lt;em&gt;If you would like to comment on this video or anything else you have seen on Future, head over to our &lt;/em&gt;&lt;a href=\"https://www.facebook.com/BBCFuture\"&gt;&lt;em&gt;Facebook&lt;/em&gt;&lt;/a&gt;&lt;em&gt; or &lt;/em&gt;&lt;a href=\"https://plus.google.com/u/0/b/107828172298602173375/107828172298602173375/posts\"&gt;&lt;em&gt;Google+&lt;/em&gt;&lt;/a&gt;&lt;em&gt; page, or message us on &lt;/em&gt;&lt;a href=\"https://twitter.com/BBC_Future\"&gt;&lt;em&gt;Twitter&lt;/em&gt;&lt;/a&gt;&lt;em&gt;.&lt;/em&gt;&lt;/p&gt;","BusinessUnit":"worldwide","CalloutBody":"","CalloutPosition":"","CalloutSubtitle":"","CalloutTitle":"","Campaign":null,"Collection":[],"DisableAdverts":false,"DisplayDate":"2014-02-21T00:00:00Z","HeadlineLong":"Body bacteria: Can your gut bugs make you smarter?","HeadlineShort":"Can gut bugs make you smarter?","HideRelated":false,"Intro":"The bacteria in our guts can influence the working of the mind, says Frank Swain. So could they be upgraded to enhance brainpower? ","IsSyndicated":false,"Location":null,"Partner":null,"PrimaryVertical":"wwfuture","Programme":null,"RelatedStory":[],"RelatedTag":null,"StoryType":"image","SummaryLong":"The bacteria in our guts can influence the working of the mind, says Frank Swain. So could they be upgraded to enhance brainpower?","SummaryShort":"Feed your body bacteria, boost brainpower","Tag":[]},"Metadata":{"CreationDateTime":"2014-11-18T09:15:29Z","Entity":"story","Guid":"27b8fbd4-9c63-45a1-a43b-df10dff960f3","Id":"wwfuture/story/20140221-can-gut-bugs-make-you-smarter","ModifiedDateTime":"2014-11-18T09:15:29Z","Project":"wwfuture","Slug":"20140221-can-gut-bugs-make-you-smarter"},"Urn":"urn:pubstack:jative:story:wwfuture/story/20140221-can-gut-bugs-make-you-smarter","_id":"559a562b0b808738c4e2f6ed"},{"Content":{"AssetCustom":"","AssetIbroadcast":null,"AssetImage":[],"AssetImagePromo":null,"AssetInfographic":"","AssetInline":null,"AssetSelect":"mps","AssetVideoIb2":null,"AssetVideoMps":[],"Author":[],"BodyHtml":"&lt;p&gt;&lt;em&gt;Listen: What does red or green sound like? In the clip above from &lt;/em&gt;&lt;a href=\"http://www.bbc.co.uk/programmes/b03nt1st\"&gt;&lt;em&gt;BBC Radio 4&rsquo;s Hack My Hearing&lt;/em&gt;&lt;/a&gt;&lt;em&gt;, Frank Swain meets an artist who created a unique device allowing him to hear colours. &lt;/em&gt;&lt;/p&gt;&lt;p&gt;Last year I became a cyborg. At the time it didn&rsquo;t seem like an auspicious occasion, more a humbling and disorientating experience. But I&rsquo;ve become excited about being part-robot.&lt;/p&gt;&lt;p&gt;My journey began when my hearing started to falter, due to a combination of unlucky genetics and too many late nights in loud clubs. By the time I was 30, I was losing scraps of conversation in crowded bars, and trips to the movies were nothing but booms and rumbles. Eventually, I relented and booked an appointment with an audiologist, who recommended I be fitted with hearing aids.&lt;/p&gt;&lt;p&gt;With that decision, I joined the millions of people whose &lt;a href=\"http://www.bbc.co.uk/news/health-17153052\"&gt;mind, body or senses are replaced by technology&lt;/a&gt;, from wireless pacemakers to bionic legs. We live in the age of augmentation, and soon &lt;a href=\"http://www.ted.com/talks/amber_case_we_are_all_cyborgs_now.html\"&gt;we may all choose to be enhanced&lt;/a&gt; in some way. After all, many prosthetic technologies do more than just fill in for our body or mind when it falls short &ndash; they now offer the potential to become &ldquo;better than human&rdquo;.&lt;/p&gt;&lt;p&gt;When I was fitted with hearing aids, I wondered: could I hack them to give me enhanced listening abilities? I explored this question in a &lt;a href=\"http://www.bbc.co.uk/programmes/b03nt1st\"&gt;BBC radio documentary&lt;/a&gt; this week, and discovered that the answer is far from simple. It turns out I don&rsquo;t actually own my new ears in the way that I thought &ndash; and this raises important questions about many other augmenting technologies on the horizon, from retinal implants to bionic arms.&lt;strong&gt;&lt;/p&gt;&lt;p&gt;&lt;/strong&gt;Unlike glasses, which simply focus the world through a lens, hearing aids take a very active role as an augment. They monitor the environment with their tiny microphones, constantly adjusting their output based on what they think is useful sound rather than noise. What I hear is their interpretation of the world around me.&lt;/p&gt;&lt;p&gt;This means these augments offer a very exciting possibility, because I don&rsquo;t have to settle for hearing that is as good as an ordinary person. I could configure my own devices to extend my senses beyond normal abilities. I wouldn&rsquo;t be first to hope for such a thing either: artist Neil Harbisson, for example, has built &lt;a href=\"http://www.bbc.co.uk/programmes/p01p6k4c\"&gt;devices that let him hear what colour sounds like&lt;/a&gt;. In my case, connecting my hearing aids to an internet-linked tool such as a smartphone, any conceivable information can be streamed directly to my hearing aids. Unlike your eyes, which can only focus on a single object, your ears are purpose-built to absorb huge amounts of complex information at once. My ears could be aware of the entire world, everything from approaching bad weather to levels of internet traffic around me. With the right app, instead of being hard of hearing, I could be superhuman.&lt;/p&gt;&lt;p&gt;&lt;strong&gt;No tinkering&lt;/strong&gt;&lt;/p&gt;&lt;p&gt;Unfortunately, supercharging my hearing aids is not just challenging, it&rsquo;s positively forbidden. During one fitting, I asked the technician calibrating them how I could adjust the settings myself, in case I found them too loud or too quiet for a particular environment. &ldquo;You can&rsquo;t do that!&rdquo; he exclaimed with some alarm. &ldquo;It&rsquo;s very important they are only set up by a qualified audiologist.&rdquo;&lt;/p&gt;&lt;p&gt;He needn&rsquo;t have worried too much. Hearing aids are, by design, incredibly resistant to tinkering. Some have a button to switch between modes for different environments. Others &ndash; like my current pair &ndash; are entirely automated, relegating me a passive listener rather than an engaged user. Traditionally designed with elderly (and presumably technophobic) customers in mind, the emphasis for manufacturers has been on invisibility and ease of use, rather than fine control. All the same, manufacturers take a dim view of users fiddling with their own devices, and it&rsquo;s very difficult for anyone who isn&rsquo;t a certified audiologist to get their hands on the specialist programming equipment. Even the peripherals, such as additional microphones or Bluetooth adaptors, tend to come locked down in proprietary formats.&lt;/p&gt;&lt;p&gt;These restrictions raise an important question: exactly who owns my hearing?&lt;/p&gt;&lt;p&gt;In the UK, any medical device implanted into the body becomes the property of that person, and even if it is subsequently removed it remains part of their estate. (Yes, in Britain you could, in theory, inherit your grandmother&rsquo;s hip replacement). However this rule doesn&rsquo;t include prosthetic devices offered by the National Health Service such as false limbs, which like crutches or a wheelchair must be returned if the patient no longer needs them.&lt;/p&gt;&lt;p&gt;Things are a little more complicated in the US, where the ownership of medical implants is likely to be governed by a patient&rsquo;s contract with their insurer. But as a 2007 article in the Journal of Medical Ethics suggested, &lt;a href=\"http://www.ncbi.nlm.nih.gov/pmc/articles/PMC2598199/#!po=12.5000\"&gt;ownership does not necessarily mean sovereignty&lt;/a&gt;, stating &ldquo;by consenting to having an implantable device placed, the patient is indirectly giving up the right to autonomous control of the device.&rdquo; &lt;strong&gt;&lt;br /&gt; &lt;/strong&gt;&lt;br /&gt; Technically, my hearing aids are only on loan from the NHS. But these devices are a part of me, an extension of myself. So should health services &ndash; or even manufacturers &ndash; be allowed to control the abilities of devices that become part of a person&rsquo;s body? In becoming a cyborg, my body has become the locus of three different parties, each of whom have different priorities over how my cybernetic hearing should function. A combination of personal, health and business interests all go into a shaping something that will become part of me.&lt;/p&gt;&lt;p&gt;Similar restrictions will apply to those hoping to use other augmentations and technological implants in the future. We can expect serious legal, societal and ethical issues to be raised as these technologies become more common. For example, should there be a limit placed on how strong a bionic arm is? Should someone with retinal implants be allowed to record the people they meet? Is it okay for someone boosting their brainpower with magnetic pulses to beat other candidates in an exam or job interview?&lt;/p&gt;&lt;p&gt;The answers are unclear so far, but what&rsquo;s more certain is that humans will continually strive to enhance themselves and exploit technology to run faster, see further or think sharper. I&rsquo;m a cyborg, and soon you may be too &ndash; but don&rsquo;t assume the transformation will be smooth.&lt;/p&gt;&lt;p&gt;&lt;em&gt;To hear more about designers and hackers augmenting their auditory abilities, listen to &lt;/em&gt;&lt;em&gt;&lt;a href=\"http://www.bbc.co.uk/programmes/b03nt1st\"&gt;BBC Radio 4&rsquo;s programme Hack My Hearing&lt;/a&gt;.&lt;/em&gt;&lt;/p&gt;&lt;p&gt;&lt;em&gt;If you would like to comment on this, or anything else you have seen on Future, head over to our &lt;/em&gt;&lt;a href=\"https://www.facebook.com/BBCFuture\"&gt;&lt;em&gt;Facebook&lt;/em&gt;&lt;/a&gt;&lt;em&gt; or &lt;/em&gt;&lt;a href=\"https://plus.google.com/u/0/b/107828172298602173375/107828172298602173375/posts\"&gt;&lt;em&gt;Google+&lt;/em&gt;&lt;/a&gt;&lt;em&gt; page, or message us on &lt;/em&gt;&lt;a href=\"https://twitter.com/BBC_Future\"&gt;&lt;em&gt;Twitter&lt;/em&gt;&lt;/a&gt;&lt;em&gt;.&lt;/em&gt;&lt;/p&gt;","BusinessUnit":"worldwide","CalloutBody":"","CalloutPosition":"","CalloutSubtitle":"","CalloutTitle":"","Campaign":null,"Collection":[],"DisableAdverts":false,"DisplayDate":"2014-01-08T00:00:00Z","HeadlineLong":"Beyond human: How I became a cyborg","HeadlineShort":"How I became a cyborg","HideRelated":false,"Intro":"When writer Frank Swain joined the ranks of the cyborgs, he discovered that it meant losing control of a part of his body. In the first of our Beyond Human series, he explores why enhancing the senses raises surprising personal and ethical problems.","IsSyndicated":false,"Location":null,"Partner":null,"PrimaryVertical":"wwfuture","Programme":null,"RelatedStory":[],"RelatedTag":null,"StoryType":"video","SummaryLong":"When writer Frank Swain joined the ranks of the cyborgs, he discovered that it meant losing control of a part of his body.","SummaryShort":"What it takes to gain super-senses","Tag":[]},"Metadata":{"CreationDateTime":"2014-11-18T09:13:32Z","Entity":"story","Guid":"f7603508-1208-4689-989e-7290685e63ca","Id":"wwfuture/story/20140107-how-i-became-a-cyborg","ModifiedDateTime":"2014-11-18T09:13:32Z","Project":"wwfuture","Slug":"20140107-how-i-became-a-cyborg"},"Urn":"urn:pubstack:jative:story:wwfuture/story/20140107-how-i-became-a-cyborg","_id":"559a56270b808738c4e2f64b"},{"Content":{"AssetCustom":"","AssetIbroadcast":[],"AssetImage":[],"AssetImagePromo":null,"AssetInfographic":"","AssetInline":null,"AssetSelect":"gallery","AssetVideoIb2":null,"AssetVideoMps":null,"Author":[],"BodyHtml":"&lt;p&gt;When Jozef Metelka gets up in the morning, one of his first decisions is which leg he&rsquo;ll be wearing that day. After losing his right leg in a motorbike accident in 2009, the keen athlete has built up a collection of 12 specialist prosthetics designed to do everything from mountain biking to snowboarding.&lt;/p&gt;&lt;p&gt;Metelka&rsquo;s first prosthetic leg was a basic model from the UK&rsquo;s National Health Service, which allowed him to stand and walk, but was &ldquo;nothing special&rdquo;. Then he met with specialists at Pace Rehabilitation in the UK, and his collection began to grow. The prostheticists at Pace began designing a suite of limbs to get him up and running &ndash; and skiing, and biking, and rollerblading. Each time Metelka suggested a new sport, the team designed a corresponding leg.&lt;/p&gt;&lt;p&gt;Creating a prosthetic that performs in all situations with the skill and flexibility of a human limb is, at the moment, not possible. Instead, prosthetics are created with specific goals in mind. Each of Metelka&rsquo;s legs arrives with a particular design challenge. Weight, strength and durability must all be balanced, as well as considerations about the environment: water, mud, and ice. For downhill mountain biking, the engineers designed a leg that could pivot at the ankle and included a robust shock absorber that could endure the heavy drops down steep hillsides. By contrast, Metelka&rsquo;s road racing leg is a streamlined flute made from a single piece of carbon fibre, lightweight and very stiff. It was even tested in a wind tunnel.&lt;/p&gt;&lt;p&gt;Metelka is not the only person to covet tailor-made prosthetics. In recent years, an abundance of specialised limbs have emerged, each as individual as the person using them. Farmer &lt;a href=\"http://modernfarmer.com/2014/01/future-farmer-prosthetics/\"&gt;David Blum created a special hard-wearing prosthetic leg&lt;/a&gt; that could meet the demands of his job, for example, and when motorsport champion Mike Shultz realised his prosthesis wouldn&rsquo;t stand up to the rigours of racing, he &lt;a href=\"http://www.wired.co.uk/magazine/archive/2013/03/start/self-made-sportsman\"&gt;built his own leg out of bike parts&lt;/a&gt;.&lt;/p&gt;&lt;p&gt;Designing something that performs on par with a living leg is no mean feat. While on a skiing trip, the freezing conditions caused the titanium bolt in Metelka&rsquo;s prosthetic to seize, and it had to be cut in two to release the socket and allow a different leg to be attached.&nbsp; But the challenge of building specialist limbs also comes with a certain freedom. Unlike natural limbs, there is no need to compromise for general use. While we can&rsquo;t build an all-purpose prosthetic as good as a regular limb, it&rsquo;s very possible to create specialised prosthetics that outperform flesh and blood in their particular category.&lt;/p&gt;&lt;p&gt;&ldquo;You can&rsquo;t do everything to the high level as a regular leg can,&rdquo; says Metelka. But for specialised legs, he admits that &ldquo;we are on the verge of building prosthetics that can outcompete natural limbs&rdquo;. Ahead of the 2012 Olympics, for example, &lt;a href=\"http://www.newscientist.com/article/dn13933-why-the-blade-runner-is-back-on-track.html#.UuDtCPvLdpg\"&gt;sport governing bodies and biomechanists &lt;/a&gt;&lt;a href=\"http://www.newscientist.com/article/dn13933-why-the-blade-runner-is-back-on-track.html#.UuDtCPvLdpg\"&gt;debated&lt;/a&gt; whether the prosthetic legs worn by sprinter Oscar Pistorius gave him an unfair advantage against able-bodied athletes. Studies suggested they didn&rsquo;t, but in five years time, says Metelka, we might even see the emergence of particular sports where able-bodied athletes can no longer hold their own against those who can wear &ndash; and can afford &ndash; high tech prosthesis. &nbsp;&nbsp;&nbsp;&lt;/p&gt;&lt;p&gt;&lt;strong&gt;Out on a limb&lt;/strong&gt;&lt;/p&gt;&lt;p&gt;Paul Carter is another multiple prosthesis user, albeit with a more modest collection. Born without arms or legs, he owns three pairs of artificial legs, including a set of running blades, the kind made famous by Pistorius. &ldquo;My blades are designed for a very specific use: running. They are brilliant for what they are, but they&rsquo;re not practical to wear every day,&rdquo; he says. The carbon fibre blades act like springs, which means it&rsquo;s almost impossible to stand still while wearing them. He jokes that the continual hopping from one foot to another makes him look like he needs the toilet.&lt;/p&gt;&lt;p&gt;When he was young, hospital staff and occupation therapists gave Carter a dizzying array of different prosthetics, each designed for a particular task. &ldquo;I had a tool for everything you can imagine, undoing buttons, putting on trousers, eating, for everything. But as I grew up and got independent, it&rsquo;s another thing to carry them around. It was like being Robin Hood, having to carry around a quiver of various sticks and rods.&rdquo; Eventually, Carter decided that he&rsquo;d rather learn to do things himself than have to rely on a multitude of arms. Both he and Metelka talk about the need to plan ahead to ensure you have the right legs with you for whatever the day holds.&lt;/p&gt;&lt;p&gt;The advent of improved materials such as titanium, carbon fibre and silicone rubber has opened up the world of prosthetics, allowing them to be made lighter, stronger, and better fitting. As well as specialising prosthetics for particular uses, it&rsquo;s also increasingly popular to customise general-use limbs: from ultra-realistic skins that include hair and tattoos, to more abstract forms. &ldquo;People tend to go two ways,&rdquo; says Carter, &ldquo;some want very realistic limbs, but many more are embracing the fact that they look different, and want to accentuate that fact.&rdquo;&lt;/p&gt;&lt;p&gt;The undisputed queen of personalised prosthetics is Sophie de Oliveira Barata, who runs the Alternative Limb Project. Her studio has produced some of the most unique and eye-catching designs in the world, incorporating &lt;a href=\"http://www.nytimes.com/interactive/2013/11/03/magazine/03limbs_in.html\"&gt;scales, magnifying glasses, laser pointers, and ultra-realistic&lt;/a&gt; features such as freckles (see gallery). For the closing ceremony of the Paralympics, she crafted model Viktoria Modesta a baroque, jewelled leg decked with &lt;a href=\"http://www.thealternativelimbproject.com/#/crystallized-leg/4570775453\"&gt;crystals and mirrors&lt;/a&gt;. Even fashion designers are getting in the act &ndash; Alexander McQueen designer a pair of intricately carved &lt;a href=\"http://blog.metmuseum.org/alexandermcqueen/tag/no-13/\"&gt;wooden legs for Paralympic athlete Aimee Mullins&lt;/a&gt;. Made without any joints, there weren&rsquo;t very practical &ndash; but that&rsquo;s hardly an unusual criticism for catwalk creations.&lt;/p&gt;&lt;p&gt;No prosthetic comes cheap. Currently running blades demand a cool $4,000 apiece, while Metelka&rsquo;s most hi-tech leg &ndash; a motorised walking foot &ndash; is an eye-watering $90,000. This high cost is the major issue for those wanting to specialise their limbs, says Carter. &ldquo;If money wasn&rsquo;t an issue I&rsquo;d get all the legs I could have,&rdquo; he laughs. &ldquo;I&rsquo;d be the Imelda Marcos of prosthetics.&rdquo;&lt;/p&gt;&lt;p&gt;&lt;em&gt;If you would like to comment on this, or anything else you have seen on Future, head over to our &lt;/em&gt;&lt;a href=\"https://www.facebook.com/BBCFuture\"&gt;&lt;em&gt;Facebook&lt;/em&gt;&lt;/a&gt;&lt;em&gt; or &lt;/em&gt;&lt;a href=\"https://plus.google.com/u/0/b/107828172298602173375/107828172298602173375/posts\"&gt;&lt;em&gt;Google+&lt;/em&gt;&lt;/a&gt;&lt;em&gt; page, or message us on &lt;/em&gt;&lt;a href=\"https://twitter.com/BBC_Future\"&gt;&lt;em&gt;Twitter&lt;/em&gt;&lt;/a&gt;&lt;em&gt;.&lt;/em&gt;&lt;/p&gt;","BusinessUnit":"worldwide","CalloutBody":"","CalloutPosition":"","CalloutSubtitle":"","CalloutTitle":"","Campaign":null,"Collection":[],"DisableAdverts":false,"DisplayDate":"2014-01-23T00:00:00Z","HeadlineLong":"Prosthetics: Meet the man with 13 legs","HeadlineShort":"The man with 13 legs","HideRelated":false,"Intro":"From robotic legs to laser arms, the owners of artificial limbs are no longer content with only one replacement, as Frank Swain discovers.","IsSyndicated":false,"Location":null,"Partner":null,"PrimaryVertical":"wwfuture","Programme":null,"RelatedStory":[],"RelatedTag":null,"StoryType":"image","SummaryLong":"From robotic legs to laser arms, the owners of artificial limbs are no longer content with only one replacement","SummaryShort":"Amazing limbs that do things you cant","Tag":[]},"Metadata":{"CreationDateTime":"2014-11-18T09:14:24Z","Entity":"story","Guid":"237fe63a-faa0-4df9-80e8-03b4ff0066d5","Id":"wwfuture/story/20140123-the-man-with-13-legs","ModifiedDateTime":"2014-11-18T09:14:24Z","Project":"wwfuture","Slug":"20140123-the-man-with-13-legs"},"Urn":"urn:pubstack:jative:story:wwfuture/story/20140123-the-man-with-13-legs","_id":"559a56290b808738c4e2f685"},{"Content":{"AssetCustom":"","AssetIbroadcast":null,"AssetImage":[],"AssetImagePromo":[],"AssetInfographic":"","AssetInline":[],"AssetSelect":"","AssetVideoIb2":null,"AssetVideoMps":null,"Author":[],"BodyHtml":"&lt;p&gt;The first thing Lindsay Ess remembers after waking up from nearly 12 hours of surgery was being afraid to look at her hands. &ldquo;I called them the new hands,&rdquo; she says, &ldquo;because they were still foreign, something new. I remember looking down and seeing purple nail polish on the thumb,&rdquo; she says, &ldquo;and I was suddenly even more aware of the fact that these belong to somebody who just passed away.&rdquo; Now, two-and-a-half years later, the hands are very much her own. But it hasn&rsquo;t been easy.&lt;/p&gt;&lt;p&gt;Ess, who lost her hands to an infection, is the beneficiary of a futuristic surgical procedure. Only 70 people around the world have received a full new replacement hand. Ess is one of the few to have two. &ldquo;This is the first time in the history of body parts that we&rsquo;ve been able to restore body parts rather than reconstruct them,&rdquo; says Dr Scott Levin, Ess&rsquo;s surgeon and the director of the Penn Hand Transplant Program. So what&rsquo;s it like to be a recipient?&lt;/p&gt;&lt;p&gt;Hand transplants are rare, and not everybody is a good candidate. Candidates have to go through a battery of tests to make sure that they&rsquo;re healthy enough to not only go through with the procedure, but stay on immunosuppressant drugs for the rest of their lives to ensure their body doesn&rsquo;t reject their new body parts. &ldquo;I can't even explain all the tests they did to me,&rdquo; Ess says, &ldquo;it was days and days.&rdquo; After a year of testing and preparing, Ess was cleared to go ahead with the transplant &ndash; and then she waited.&lt;/p&gt;&lt;p&gt;One day, in late September, a donor set of hands became available. Ess entered the operating room, and with the help of 12 surgeons emerged with a new pair of hands. The procedure requires doctors to reconnect bones, muscles, tendons, nerves, arteries and veins layer by layer, using microscopes to reattach the tiny pieces. First the bones are reattached, and then blood flow is established to the replacement limb by recoupling arteries and veins. After that, the tendons, nerves and skin are all reconnected. Microsurgery of this kind has been used for years to reattach fingers in emergency situations, but now doctors are using it in more controlled scenarios to reattach a whole arm. Twelve hours of delicate reattachment later, and Ess had two new hands. &ldquo;And then the hard work started,&rdquo; she says.&lt;/p&gt;&lt;p&gt;{\"image\":{\"pid\":\"p01w8h95\"}}&lt;/p&gt;&lt;p&gt;Ess spent months in therapy &ndash; five hours a day of stretching, bending, reaching, and trying to encourage her own nerves and muscles to fuse and grow into the new hands. At first, her hand therapist simply moved her hands for her, as she tried to remember what it was like to move her own fingers. &ldquo;A lot of it is mental,&rdquo; Ess says, &ldquo;because your nerves have to regenerate, but your mind also has to regenerate the thought of moving your finger.&rdquo;&lt;/p&gt;&lt;p&gt;It wasn&rsquo;t until three months after her surgery that Ess could move her new hands at all. &ldquo;I raised my arms up, just to stretch, and I looked up and them and my right pointer finger and right middle finger moved a little bit,&rdquo; she said. &ldquo;And I kind of freaked out. I thought it was a freak thing that one time, but I could do it again, and again. That might have been my first time when I was like &lsquo;wow, these are my hands&rsquo;.&rdquo; Today, two-and-a-half years after her transplant, Ess still goes to therapy once or twice a week for an hour-and-a-half. She&rsquo;ll probably go to therapy forever, she says, but it&rsquo;s worth it to regain control over her life. &ldquo;It takes a lot of persistence and diligence,&rdquo; to make this procedure successful, Ess says. &ldquo;You need a good fight inside of you.&rdquo;&lt;/p&gt;&lt;p&gt;{\"image\":{\"pid\":\"p01w8h6z\"}}&lt;/p&gt;&lt;p&gt;Having the hands has transformed her life. &ldquo;I live by myself, I have a dog, I drive, I have normal stuff that I do,&rdquo; she says. &ldquo;These transplants may not be life saving, they are certainly quality-of-life altering,&rdquo; says Levin.&lt;/p&gt;&lt;p&gt;There are still things Ess can&rsquo;t quite do with her hands, like put her hair up behind her back, or rummage around in a full purse and pull something out without looking. &ldquo;Say I want to get a chip out of a bag, I can do that,&rdquo; she says. &ldquo;But if it&rsquo;s &lt;a href=\"http://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Chex_Mix\"&gt;Chex Mix&lt;/a&gt; I can&rsquo;t pick out my favourite, the pretzels.&rdquo; That will come with practice and therapy, she says.&lt;/p&gt;&lt;p&gt;Ess says she now sees the hands as her own. Some of that has to do with re-growing the crucial nerves from her body into her hands that allow her to bend and flex her fingers. And some of it is simply the time it takes for a body to adjust to a new addition, whether it&rsquo;s prosthetic or transplanted. Research has shown that the brain can integrate apparently foreign objects into our perceived body image. Perhaps the most famous example of this is the &lt;a href=\"http://www.newscientist.com/article/mg21729052.500-the-self-trick-yourself-into-an-outofbody-experience.html#.UzL83c4Yvms\"&gt;rubber hand illusion&lt;/a&gt;, where researchers manipulate an able-bodied person&rsquo;s sense of touch and perception to induce the feeling that a fake hand is their own (see video, below). For those with the new hand actually transplanted onto them, that feeling is even more convincing.&lt;/p&gt;&lt;p&gt;{\"magnify\":{ \"id\": \"The-Rubber-Hand-Illusion-Horizo\"}}&lt;/p&gt;&lt;p&gt;Hand transplants are probably never going to be the go-to option for everybody, says Levin. &ldquo;It&rsquo;s always a trade-off. You have prostheses that break, or you have hand transplants that look and can feel normal, but you&rsquo;re taking medicine for the rest of your life to keep them from being rejected.&rdquo; Prosthetic technology is advancing alongside transplants too, and some day amputees will be able to choose between really good options to replace their lost limbs. &ldquo;It&rsquo;s always the patient&rsquo;s choice,&rdquo; says Levin.&lt;/p&gt;&lt;p&gt;Ess says she still thinks about whoever donated their body parts and helped her live her life again. But apart from that hint of nail varnish when she woke up, Ess doesn&rsquo;t know anything about that person, and it&rsquo;s likely to stay that way. &ldquo;We are very cautious about not revealing the donor to the recipient,&rdquo; Levin says. &ldquo;She doesn&rsquo;t know anything, nor is it fair for a recipient to know anything.&rdquo;&lt;/p&gt;&lt;p&gt;More than anything, Ess is grateful to her donor. She says her second thought after waking up from surgery was &ldquo;just being utterly thankful, that I survived and that somebody gave me such a gift,&rdquo; she says. And even though it&rsquo;s been hard, she&rsquo;s happy she did it. &ldquo;I don&rsquo;t have any regrets at all, not even one.&rdquo;&lt;/p&gt;&lt;p&gt;&lt;em&gt;If you would like to comment on this, or anything else you have seen on Future, head over to our &lt;/em&gt;&lt;a href=\"https://www.facebook.com/BBCFuture\"&gt;&lt;em&gt;Facebook&lt;/em&gt;&lt;/a&gt;&lt;em&gt; or &lt;/em&gt;&lt;a href=\"https://plus.google.com/u/0/b/107828172298602173375/107828172298602173375/posts\"&gt;&lt;em&gt;Google+&lt;/em&gt;&lt;/a&gt;&lt;em&gt; page, or message us on &lt;/em&gt;&lt;a href=\"https://twitter.com/BBC_Future\"&gt;&lt;em&gt;Twitter&lt;/em&gt;&lt;/a&gt;&lt;em&gt;.&lt;/em&gt;&lt;/p&gt;","BusinessUnit":"worldwide","CalloutBody":"","CalloutPosition":"","CalloutSubtitle":"","CalloutTitle":"","Campaign":null,"Collection":[],"DisableAdverts":false,"DisplayDate":"2014-03-27T00:00:00Z","HeadlineLong":"I had a double hand transplant","HeadlineShort":"I had a double hand transplant","HideRelated":false,"Intro":"Imagine looking down at your hands and seeing fingers that arent your own. Images)","TemplateUrl":"http://ichef.bbci.co.uk/wwfeatures/$recipe/images/live/p0/1w/8h/p01w8hq0.jpg","Title":"newhandindex_crop.jpg","CreationDateTime":"0001-01-01T00:00:00Z","Entity":"","Guid":"","Id":"p01w8hq0","ModifiedDateTime":"0001-01-01T00:00:00Z","Project":"","Slug":""},"Metadata":{"CreationDateTime":"0001-01-01T00:00:00Z","Entity":"","Guid":"","Id":"p01w8hq0","ModifiedDateTime":"0001-01-01T00:00:00Z","Project":"","Slug":""},"Urn":"urn:pubstack:jative:image:p01w8hq0","_id":"559a54910b808738c4e2bbe7"}],"AssetInfographic":"","AssetInline":[{"Content":{"FileSizeBytes":86923,"MimeType":"image/jpeg","SourceHeight":549,"SourceUrl":"http://deltaorigin.bbc.co.uk/images/live/p0/1w/8h/p01w8h95.jpg","SourceWidth":976,"SynopsisLong":"It took 12 surgeons to attach the new hands (ABC News)","SynopsisMedium":"It took 12 surgeons to attach the new hands (ABC News)","SynopsisShort":"It took 12 surgeons to attach the new hands (ABC News)","TemplateUrl":"http://ichef.bbci.co.uk/wwfeatures/$recipe/images/live/p0/1w/8h/p01w8h95.jpg","Title":"lindsay_ess3_ABC_crop.jpg"},"Metadata":{"CreationDateTime":"0001-01-01T00:00:00Z","Entity":"","Guid":"","Id":"p01w8h95","ModifiedDateTime":"0001-01-01T00:00:00Z","Project":"","Slug":""},"Urn":"urn:pubstack:jative:image:p01w8h95","_id":"559a54900b808738c4e2bbe6"},{"Content":{"FileSizeBytes":76239,"MimeType":"image/jpeg","SourceHeight":549,"SourceUrl":"http://deltaorigin.bbc.co.uk/images/live/p0/1w/8h/p01w8h6z.jpg","SourceWidth":976,"SynopsisLong":"Ess needed extensive physiotherapy, but gained finger movement eventually (ABC News)","SynopsisMedium":"Ess needed extensive physiotherapy, but gained finger movement eventually (ABC News)","SynopsisShort":"Ess needed extensive physiotherapy, but gained finger movement eventually (ABC News)","TemplateUrl":"http://ichef.bbci.co.uk/wwfeatures/$recipe/images/live/p0/1w/8h/p01w8h6z.jpg","Title":"lindsay_ess1_ABC_crop.jpg"},"Metadata":{"CreationDateTime":"0001-01-01T00:00:00Z","Entity":"","Guid":"","Id":"p01w8h6z","ModifiedDateTime":"0001-01-01T00:00:00Z","Project":"","Slug":""},"Urn":"urn:pubstack:jative:image:p01w8h6z","_id":"559a54900b808738c4e2bbe5"}],"AssetSelect":"","AssetVideoIb2":null,"AssetVideoMps":null,"Author":[{"Content":{"AssetImage":[],"Description":"&lt;p&gt;Rose\nEveleth explores how humans tangle with science and technology for publications like Nautilus\nMagazine, NOVA and Scientific American. You can find more of her work at her &lt;a href=\"http://roseveleth.com/\" target=\"_blank\"&gt;website&lt;/a&gt;, and you can get in\ntouch with her on Twitter &lt;a href=\"http://twitter.com/roseveleth\" target=\"_blank\"&gt;@roseveleth&lt;/a&gt;.&lt;/p&gt;","Email":[],"Links":null,"Name":"Rose Eveleth","PrimaryVertical":"wwfuture"},"Metadata":{"CreationDateTime":"2014-11-18T09:11:45Z","Entity":"author","Guid":"fc5f2ef8-a0df-4815-9f94-a2ea4ff34382","Id":"wwfuture/author/rose-eveleth","ModifiedDateTime":"2014-11-18T09:11:45Z","Project":"wwfuture","Slug":"rose-eveleth"},"Urn":"urn:pubstack:jative:author:wwfuture/author/rose-eveleth","_id":"559a524f0b808738c4e25fb9"}],"BodyHtml":"&lt;p&gt;The first thing Lindsay Ess remembers after waking up from nearly 12 hours of surgery was being afraid to look at her hands. &ldquo;I called them the new hands,&rdquo; she says, &ldquo;because they were still foreign, something new. I remember looking down and seeing purple nail polish on the thumb,&rdquo; she says, &ldquo;and I was suddenly even more aware of the fact that these belong to somebody who just passed away.&rdquo; Now, two-and-a-half years later, the hands are very much her own. But it hasn&rsquo;t been easy.&lt;/p&gt;&lt;p&gt;Ess, who lost her hands to an infection, is the beneficiary of a futuristic surgical procedure. Only 70 people around the world have received a full new replacement hand. Ess is one of the few to have two. &ldquo;This is the first time in the history of body parts that we&rsquo;ve been able to restore body parts rather than reconstruct them,&rdquo; says Dr Scott Levin, Ess&rsquo;s surgeon and the director of the Penn Hand Transplant Program. So what&rsquo;s it like to be a recipient?&lt;/p&gt;&lt;p&gt;Hand transplants are rare, and not everybody is a good candidate. Candidates have to go through a battery of tests to make sure that they&rsquo;re healthy enough to not only go through with the procedure, but stay on immunosuppressant drugs for the rest of their lives to ensure their body doesn&rsquo;t reject their new body parts. &ldquo;I can't even explain all the tests they did to me,&rdquo; Ess says, &ldquo;it was days and days.&rdquo; After a year of testing and preparing, Ess was cleared to go ahead with the transplant &ndash; and then she waited.&lt;/p&gt;&lt;p&gt;One day, in late September, a donor set of hands became available. Ess entered the operating room, and with the help of 12 surgeons emerged with a new pair of hands. The procedure requires doctors to reconnect bones, muscles, tendons, nerves, arteries and veins layer by layer, using microscopes to reattach the tiny pieces. First the bones are reattached, and then blood flow is established to the replacement limb by recoupling arteries and veins. After that, the tendons, nerves and skin are all reconnected. Microsurgery of this kind has been used for years to reattach fingers in emergency situations, but now doctors are using it in more controlled scenarios to reattach a whole arm. Twelve hours of delicate reattachment later, and Ess had two new hands. &ldquo;And then the hard work started,&rdquo; she says.&lt;/p&gt;&lt;p&gt;{\"image\":{\"pid\":\"p01w8h95\"}}&lt;/p&gt;&lt;p&gt;Ess spent months in therapy &ndash; five hours a day of stretching, bending, reaching, and trying to encourage her own nerves and muscles to fuse and grow into the new hands. At first, her hand therapist simply moved her hands for her, as she tried to remember what it was like to move her own fingers. &ldquo;A lot of it is mental,&rdquo; Ess says, &ldquo;because your nerves have to regenerate, but your mind also has to regenerate the thought of moving your finger.&rdquo;&lt;/p&gt;&lt;p&gt;It wasn&rsquo;t until three months after her surgery that Ess could move her new hands at all. &ldquo;I raised my arms up, just to stretch, and I looked up and them and my right pointer finger and right middle finger moved a little bit,&rdquo; she said. &ldquo;And I kind of freaked out. I thought it was a freak thing that one time, but I could do it again, and again. That might have been my first time when I was like &lsquo;wow, these are my hands&rsquo;.&rdquo; Today, two-and-a-half years after her transplant, Ess still goes to therapy once or twice a week for an hour-and-a-half. She&rsquo;ll probably go to therapy forever, she says, but it&rsquo;s worth it to regain control over her life. &ldquo;It takes a lot of persistence and diligence,&rdquo; to make this procedure successful, Ess says. &ldquo;You need a good fight inside of you.&rdquo;&lt;/p&gt;&lt;p&gt;{\"image\":{\"pid\":\"p01w8h6z\"}}&lt;/p&gt;&lt;p&gt;Having the hands has transformed her life. &ldquo;I live by myself, I have a dog, I drive, I have normal stuff that I do,&rdquo; she says. &ldquo;These transplants may not be life saving, they are certainly quality-of-life altering,&rdquo; says Levin.&lt;/p&gt;&lt;p&gt;There are still things Ess can&rsquo;t quite do with her hands, like put her hair up behind her back, or rummage around in a full purse and pull something out without looking. &ldquo;Say I want to get a chip out of a bag, I can do that,&rdquo; she says. &ldquo;But if it&rsquo;s &lt;a href=\"http://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Chex_Mix\"&gt;Chex Mix&lt;/a&gt; I can&rsquo;t pick out my favourite, the pretzels.&rdquo; That will come with practice and therapy, she says.&lt;/p&gt;&lt;p&gt;Ess says she now sees the hands as her own. Some of that has to do with re-growing the crucial nerves from her body into her hands that allow her to bend and flex her fingers. And some of it is simply the time it takes for a body to adjust to a new addition, whether it&rsquo;s prosthetic or transplanted. Research has shown that the brain can integrate apparently foreign objects into our perceived body image. Perhaps the most famous example of this is the &lt;a href=\"http://www.newscientist.com/article/mg21729052.500-the-self-trick-yourself-into-an-outofbody-experience.html#.UzL83c4Yvms\"&gt;rubber hand illusion&lt;/a&gt;, where researchers manipulate an able-bodied person&rsquo;s sense of touch and perception to induce the feeling that a fake hand is their own (see video, below). For those with the new hand actually transplanted onto them, that feeling is even more convincing.&lt;/p&gt;&lt;p&gt;{\"magnify\":{ \"id\": \"The-Rubber-Hand-Illusion-Horizo\"}}&lt;/p&gt;&lt;p&gt;Hand transplants are probably never going to be the go-to option for everybody, says Levin. &ldquo;It&rsquo;s always a trade-off. You have prostheses that break, or you have hand transplants that look and can feel normal, but you&rsquo;re taking medicine for the rest of your life to keep them from being rejected.&rdquo; Prosthetic technology is advancing alongside transplants too, and some day amputees will be able to choose between really good options to replace their lost limbs. &ldquo;It&rsquo;s always the patient&rsquo;s choice,&rdquo; says Levin.&lt;/p&gt;&lt;p&gt;Ess says she still thinks about whoever donated their body parts and helped her live her life again. But apart from that hint of nail varnish when she woke up, Ess doesn&rsquo;t know anything about that person, and it&rsquo;s likely to stay that way. &ldquo;We are very cautious about not revealing the donor to the recipient,&rdquo; Levin says. &ldquo;She doesn&rsquo;t know anything, nor is it fair for a recipient to know anything.&rdquo;&lt;/p&gt;&lt;p&gt;More than anything, Ess is grateful to her donor. She says her second thought after waking up from surgery was &ldquo;just being utterly thankful, that I survived and that somebody gave me such a gift,&rdquo; she says. And even though it&rsquo;s been hard, she&rsquo;s happy she did it. &ldquo;I don&rsquo;t have any regrets at all, not even one.&rdquo;&lt;/p&gt;&lt;p&gt;&lt;em&gt;If you would like to comment on this, or anything else you have seen on Future, head over to our &lt;/em&gt;&lt;a href=\"https://www.facebook.com/BBCFuture\"&gt;&lt;em&gt;Facebook&lt;/em&gt;&lt;/a&gt;&lt;em&gt; or &lt;/em&gt;&lt;a href=\"https://plus.google.com/u/0/b/107828172298602173375/107828172298602173375/posts\"&gt;&lt;em&gt;Google+&lt;/em&gt;&lt;/a&gt;&lt;em&gt; page, or message us on &lt;/em&gt;&lt;a href=\"https://twitter.com/BBC_Future\"&gt;&lt;em&gt;Twitter&lt;/em&gt;&lt;/a&gt;&lt;em&gt;.&lt;/em&gt;&lt;/p&gt;","BusinessUnit":"worldwide","CalloutBody":"","CalloutPosition":"","CalloutSubtitle":"","CalloutTitle":"","Campaign":null,"Collection":[{"Content":{"AssetImage":[],"Campaign":null,"CollectionOverrides":null,"CollectionType":"column","Description":"The people living the future today. Rose Eveleth meets those witnessing the forefront of science and technology, and their personal experiences reveal what may be possible for all of us.","Name":"Tomorrow's Lives","Partner":null,"PrimaryVertical":"wwfuture","Title":"Tomorrow's Lives","CreationDateTime":"2015-05-20T11:38:12.9879Z","Entity":"collection","Guid":"ea952ee0-acd1-4d20-8552-7275f7fbaf8f","Id":"wwfuture/column/tomorrows-lives","ModifiedDateTime":"2015-05-20T11:38:12.9879Z","Project":"wwfuture","Slug":"column/tomorrows-lives"},"Metadata":{"CreationDateTime":"2015-05-20T11:38:12.9879Z","Entity":"collection","Guid":"ea952ee0-acd1-4d20-8552-7275f7fbaf8f","Id":"wwfuture/column/tomorrows-lives","ModifiedDateTime":"2015-05-20T11:38:12.9879Z","Project":"wwfuture","Slug":"column/tomorrows-lives"},"Urn":"urn:pubstack:jative:collection:wwfuture/column/tomorrows-lives","_id":"559a52620b808738c4e262b5"},{"Content":{"AssetImage":[],"Campaign":null,"CollectionOverrides":[],"CollectionType":"section","Description":"Health","Name":"Health","Partner":null,"PrimaryVertical":"wwfuture","Title":"Health"},"Metadata":{"CreationDateTime":"2015-05-20T10:44:06.503434Z","Entity":"collection","Guid":"00a9a33a-e393-4d09-8029-7e9d5bd1d8cb","Id":"wwfuture/section/health","ModifiedDateTime":"2015-05-20T13:52:37.074442Z","Project":"wwfuture","Slug":"section/health"},"Urn":"urn:pubstack:jative:collection:wwfuture/section/health","_id":"559a52620b808738c4e262db"},{"Content":{"AssetImage":[],"Campaign":null,"CollectionOverrides":[],"CollectionType":"section","Description":"Science &amp; Environment","Name":"Science &amp; Environment","Partner":null,"PrimaryVertical":"wwfuture","Title":"Science &amp; Environment"},"Metadata":{"CreationDateTime":"2015-05-20T10:51:45.984509Z","Entity":"collection","Guid":"1aa4ccec-87b5-44b8-b5af-c553debaf6f6","Id":"wwfuture/section/science-environment","ModifiedDateTime":"2015-05-20T13:51:20.083392Z","Project":"wwfuture","Slug":"section/science-environment"},"Urn":"urn:pubstack:jative:collection:wwfuture/section/science-environment","_id":"559a52620b808738c4e262dc"},{"Content":{"AssetImage":[],"Campaign":null,"CollectionOverrides":[],"CollectionType":"section","Description":"Technology","Name":"Technology","Partner":null,"PrimaryVertical":"wwfuture","Title":"Technology"},"Metadata":{"CreationDateTime":"2015-05-20T11:42:22.339015Z","Entity":"collection","Guid":"fadfa439-a201-46b6-a8fd-d8813cac4bbf","Id":"wwfuture/section/technology","ModifiedDateTime":"2015-05-20T13:49:02.740858Z","Project":"wwfuture","Slug":"section/technology"},"Urn":"urn:pubstack:jative:collection:wwfuture/section/technology","_id":"559a52620b808738c4e262dd"}],"DisableAdverts":false,"DisplayDate":"2014-03-27T00:00:00Z","HeadlineLong":"I had a double hand transplant","HeadlineShort":"I had a double hand transplant","HideRelated":false,"Intro":"Imagine looking down at your hands and seeing fingers that arent your own. Rose Eveleth asks a woman with a double hand transplant what it feels like. Its the first in a series where we talk to fascinating people who are living the future today.","IsSyndicated":true,"Location":null,"Partner":null,"PrimaryVertical":"wwfuture","Programme":null,"RelatedStory":[{"Content":{"AssetCustom":"","AssetIbroadcast":null,"AssetImage":[],"AssetImagePromo":null,"AssetInfographic":"","AssetInline":[],"AssetSelect":"","AssetVideoIb2":null,"AssetVideoMps":null,"Author":[],"BodyHtml":"&lt;p&gt;Under the watchful eye of the prison guards at Metro Davidson County Detention Facility, half a dozen inmates in blue overalls are wrestling with prosthetic legs. They strip each one down into a collection of screws, bolts, connectors, feet and other components. The prison workshop is home to a collaboration with &lt;a href=\"http://www.standingwithhope.com/\"&gt;Standing With Hope&lt;/a&gt;, a US charity based in Nashville, Tennessee that recycles unwanted prosthetic limbs for the developing world. The disassembled legs will be shipped to Ghana, where locally trained clinicians will rebuild them to fit patients there.&lt;/p&gt;&lt;p&gt;These legs will get a second life, but other types of prosthetics and implants usually face a different destiny. What to do with augmented human parts when they are no longer needed &ndash; often due to the owner&rsquo;s death &ndash; is an increasingly common issue. Modern medicine offers a litany of replacement parts, from whole limbs to metal hips, shoulders and joints. Then there are pacemakers and internal cardiac defibrillators (ICDs), as well as more common augmentations like dentures and silicone breast implants. What happens to these augmentations when someone dies?&lt;/p&gt;&lt;p&gt;{\"image\":{\"pid\":\"p01tld5l\"}}&lt;/p&gt;&lt;p&gt;Inert devices such as breast implants and replacement hips tend not to be removed after death, largely because there&rsquo;s no compelling reason to do so, and they pose little threat to the environment. So it&rsquo;s likely that the archaeologists of future centuries will uncover peculiar objects in the graves of the millennial dead: silicone bags, plastic teeth and sculpted metal bones.&lt;/p&gt;&lt;p&gt;It&rsquo;s a different story for cremation. In a furnace, silicone may burn up, but not the metal in implants &ndash; such as titanium or cobalt alloy. It is often separated from the ash and disposed of separately. Even tiny amounts of precious metals such as gold fillings can be discovered by waving a metal-detector over the ashes.&lt;/p&gt;&lt;p&gt;{\"image\":{\"pid\":\"p01tlcr7\"}}&lt;/p&gt;&lt;p&gt;In recent years, enterprising organisations have stepped in to recycle this material. Dutch company Orthometals, for example, collects 250 tonnes of metal every year from hundreds of crematoriums around Europe. At their facility in Steenbergen, it is sorted and melted down into ingots before being sold to the automobile and aeronautical industries. A similar US company, Implant Recycling, sells the melted and recast metals back into the medical industry. After you die, a little piece of you may one day end up in an aeroplane, a wind turbine, or even another person.&lt;/p&gt;&lt;p&gt;Pacemakers and ICDs, by contrast, are often taken out of the body after death &ndash; and almost always before cremation, because the batteries can explode when heated. The same goes for spinal cord stimulators that treat pain and some types of internal pumps for administering drugs, since they contain electronics too.&lt;br /&gt; &nbsp;&lt;br /&gt; Once removed, implants are typically discarded &ndash; both the European Union and the US, among others, have rules that forbid the reuse of implanted medical devices. However, there is a growing trend to recover them for use in the developing world.&lt;/p&gt;&lt;p&gt;{\"image\":{\"pid\":\"p01tlcvl\"}}&lt;/p&gt;&lt;p&gt;At $4,000 for a pacemaker and $20,000 for an ICD, a second-hand implant is the only way that millions of people will be able to afford this life-saving equipment. In the UK, charity &lt;a href=\"http://www.pace4life.org/\"&gt;Pace4Life&lt;/a&gt; collects functioning pacemakers from funeral parlours for use in India. In a similar effort, the journal Annals of Internal Medicine recently published the results of a US programme called Project My Heart Your Heart, which found that 75 patients who received second-hand ICDs showed no evidence of infection or malfunction. The group are now applying for FDA approval to send recycled heart devices overseas.&lt;/p&gt;&lt;p&gt;Back in Nashville, Standing With Hope has adopted a similar approach by shipping prosthetic limbs to Ghana. The charity&rsquo;s co-founder, Gracie Rosenberger, was badly injured in a traffic accident at 17, an incident which cost her both legs. Like many amputees, Gracie acquired a stockpile of prosthetics over the years, which made her wonder whether they could be put to better use. As limbs are replaced or outgrown, the old ones gather dust in the backs of closets. When an amputee passes away, the family are often left with a cache of working limbs but no one to take them.&lt;/p&gt;&lt;p&gt;&ldquo;The private insurers do not want it back, I don&rsquo;t even think Medicare wants it back,&rdquo; explains Rosenberger&rsquo;s husband Peter, who is president of Standing With Hope. &ldquo;There are all kinds of liabilities. So a lot of this stuff is discarded, unfortunately.&rdquo;&lt;/p&gt;&lt;p&gt;Now amputees and their families can send old limbs in the mail to the Rosenbergers. When asking for donations, Standing With Hope&rsquo;s website reads: &ldquo;We&rsquo;re not asking for an arm and a leg... just a leg&rdquo;.&lt;/p&gt;&lt;p&gt;{\"image\":{\"pid\":\"p01tldc8\"}}&lt;/p&gt;&lt;p&gt;The goal is to beat last year&rsquo;s total of 500 replacement limbs delivered to Ghana. &ldquo;Last year I had a thing I called Operation Footloose, and on my radio show I would play the theme from Footloose and say &lsquo;turn that foot loose so we can recycle it&rsquo;,&rdquo; Peter laughs.&lt;/p&gt;&lt;p&gt;Just like organ donors, those that bequeath their medical implants can bid farewell to the world with the knowledge they offer a stranger a second chance at life, be it a man with a heart defect in India, a woman undergoing a hip replacement in America, or a child with a missing limb in Ghana. And it&rsquo;s not just donors and recipients that have something to gain from the process. The Metro Davidson County Detention Facility is just a few minutes&rsquo; drive from Peter&rsquo;s home, and every so often he visits the inmates working in the limb disassembly workshop. As they chatted, one prisoner told Peter what the Standing With Hope project meant to him. &ldquo;He had tears in his eyes and said to me: &lsquo;I get to do something positive for the first time with my hands. I&rsquo;ve never done anything positive with my hands&rsquo;,&rdquo; Peter recalls. &ldquo;How rewarding is that?&rdquo;&lt;/p&gt;&lt;p&gt;&lt;em&gt;If you would like to comment on this, or anything else you have seen on Future, head over to our &lt;/em&gt;&lt;a href=\"https://www.facebook.com/BBCFuture\"&gt;&lt;em&gt;Facebook&lt;/em&gt;&lt;/a&gt;&lt;em&gt; or &lt;/em&gt;&lt;a href=\"https://plus.google.com/u/0/b/107828172298602173375/107828172298602173375/posts\"&gt;&lt;em&gt;Google+&lt;/em&gt;&lt;/a&gt;&lt;em&gt; page, or message us on &lt;/em&gt;&lt;a href=\"https://twitter.com/BBC_Future\"&gt;&lt;em&gt;Twitter&lt;/em&gt;&lt;/a&gt;&lt;em&gt;.&lt;/em&gt;&lt;/p&gt;","BusinessUnit":"worldwide","CalloutBody":"","CalloutPosition":"","CalloutSubtitle":"","CalloutTitle":"","Campaign":null,"Collection":[],"DisableAdverts":false,"DisplayDate":"2014-03-11T00:00:00Z","HeadlineLong":"What happens to prosthetics and implants after you die?","HeadlineShort":"Body parts that live after death","HideRelated":false,"Intro":"Millions of prosthetics, breast implants, and pacemakers now exist  so what happens to all these augmentations when their owners die or no longer need them? Frank Swain investigates.","IsSyndicated":true,"Location":null,"Partner":null,"PrimaryVertical":"wwfuture","Programme":null,"RelatedStory":[],"RelatedTag":null,"StoryType":"image","SummaryLong":"Millions of people are fitted with prosthetics, breast implants, and pacemakers  so what happens to them when their owners die? Frank Swain investigates","SummaryShort":"What happens to prosthetics when you die?","Tag":[]},"Metadata":{"CreationDateTime":"2014-11-18T09:14:56Z","Entity":"story","Guid":"26641d54-39eb-43e0-956f-5a12c99370e3","Id":"wwfuture/story/20140311-body-parts-that-live-after-death","ModifiedDateTime":"2014-11-18T09:14:56Z","Project":"wwfuture","Slug":"20140311-body-parts-that-live-after-death"},"Urn":"urn:pubstack:jative:story:wwfuture/story/20140311-body-parts-that-live-after-death","_id":"559a562d0b808738c4e2f71f"},{"Content":{"AssetCustom":"","AssetIbroadcast":[],"AssetImage":[],"AssetImagePromo":null,"AssetInfographic":"","AssetInline":null,"AssetSelect":"gallery","AssetVideoIb2":null,"AssetVideoMps":null,"Author":[],"BodyHtml":"&lt;p&gt;When Jozef Metelka gets up in the morning, one of his first decisions is which leg he&rsquo;ll be wearing that day. After losing his right leg in a motorbike accident in 2009, the keen athlete has built up a collection of 12 specialist prosthetics designed to do everything from mountain biking to snowboarding.&lt;/p&gt;&lt;p&gt;Metelka&rsquo;s first prosthetic leg was a basic model from the UK&rsquo;s National Health Service, which allowed him to stand and walk, but was &ldquo;nothing special&rdquo;. Then he met with specialists at Pace Rehabilitation in the UK, and his collection began to grow. The prostheticists at Pace began designing a suite of limbs to get him up and running &ndash; and skiing, and biking, and rollerblading. Each time Metelka suggested a new sport, the team designed a corresponding leg.&lt;/p&gt;&lt;p&gt;Creating a prosthetic that performs in all situations with the skill and flexibility of a human limb is, at the moment, not possible. Instead, prosthetics are created with specific goals in mind. Each of Metelka&rsquo;s legs arrives with a particular design challenge. Weight, strength and durability must all be balanced, as well as considerations about the environment: water, mud, and ice. For downhill mountain biking, the engineers designed a leg that could pivot at the ankle and included a robust shock absorber that could endure the heavy drops down steep hillsides. By contrast, Metelka&rsquo;s road racing leg is a streamlined flute made from a single piece of carbon fibre, lightweight and very stiff. It was even tested in a wind tunnel.&lt;/p&gt;&lt;p&gt;Metelka is not the only person to covet tailor-made prosthetics. In recent years, an abundance of specialised limbs have emerged, each as individual as the person using them. Farmer &lt;a href=\"http://modernfarmer.com/2014/01/future-farmer-prosthetics/\"&gt;David Blum created a special hard-wearing prosthetic leg&lt;/a&gt; that could meet the demands of his job, for example, and when motorsport champion Mike Shultz realised his prosthesis wouldn&rsquo;t stand up to the rigours of racing, he &lt;a href=\"http://www.wired.co.uk/magazine/archive/2013/03/start/self-made-sportsman\"&gt;built his own leg out of bike parts&lt;/a&gt;.&lt;/p&gt;&lt;p&gt;Designing something that performs on par with a living leg is no mean feat. While on a skiing trip, the freezing conditions caused the titanium bolt in Metelka&rsquo;s prosthetic to seize, and it had to be cut in two to release the socket and allow a different leg to be attached.&nbsp; But the challenge of building specialist limbs also comes with a certain freedom. Unlike natural limbs, there is no need to compromise for general use. While we can&rsquo;t build an all-purpose prosthetic as good as a regular limb, it&rsquo;s very possible to create specialised prosthetics that outperform flesh and blood in their particular category.&lt;/p&gt;&lt;p&gt;&ldquo;You can&rsquo;t do everything to the high level as a regular leg can,&rdquo; says Metelka. But for specialised legs, he admits that &ldquo;we are on the verge of building prosthetics that can outcompete natural limbs&rdquo;. Ahead of the 2012 Olympics, for example, &lt;a href=\"http://www.newscientist.com/article/dn13933-why-the-blade-runner-is-back-on-track.html#.UuDtCPvLdpg\"&gt;sport governing bodies and biomechanists &lt;/a&gt;&lt;a href=\"http://www.newscientist.com/article/dn13933-why-the-blade-runner-is-back-on-track.html#.UuDtCPvLdpg\"&gt;debated&lt;/a&gt; whether the prosthetic legs worn by sprinter Oscar Pistorius gave him an unfair advantage against able-bodied athletes. Studies suggested they didn&rsquo;t, but in five years time, says Metelka, we might even see the emergence of particular sports where able-bodied athletes can no longer hold their own against those who can wear &ndash; and can afford &ndash; high tech prosthesis. &nbsp;&nbsp;&nbsp;&lt;/p&gt;&lt;p&gt;&lt;strong&gt;Out on a limb&lt;/strong&gt;&lt;/p&gt;&lt;p&gt;Paul Carter is another multiple prosthesis user, albeit with a more modest collection. Born without arms or legs, he owns three pairs of artificial legs, including a set of running blades, the kind made famous by Pistorius. &ldquo;My blades are designed for a very specific use: running. They are brilliant for what they are, but they&rsquo;re not practical to wear every day,&rdquo; he says. The carbon fibre blades act like springs, which means it&rsquo;s almost impossible to stand still while wearing them. He jokes that the continual hopping from one foot to another makes him look like he needs the toilet.&lt;/p&gt;&lt;p&gt;When he was young, hospital staff and occupation therapists gave Carter a dizzying array of different prosthetics, each designed for a particular task. &ldquo;I had a tool for everything you can imagine, undoing buttons, putting on trousers, eating, for everything. But as I grew up and got independent, it&rsquo;s another thing to carry them around. It was like being Robin Hood, having to carry around a quiver of various sticks and rods.&rdquo; Eventually, Carter decided that he&rsquo;d rather learn to do things himself than have to rely on a multitude of arms. Both he and Metelka talk about the need to plan ahead to ensure you have the right legs with you for whatever the day holds.&lt;/p&gt;&lt;p&gt;The advent of improved materials such as titanium, carbon fibre and silicone rubber has opened up the world of prosthetics, allowing them to be made lighter, stronger, and better fitting. As well as specialising prosthetics for particular uses, it&rsquo;s also increasingly popular to customise general-use limbs: from ultra-realistic skins that include hair and tattoos, to more abstract forms. &ldquo;People tend to go two ways,&rdquo; says Carter, &ldquo;some want very realistic limbs, but many more are embracing the fact that they look different, and want to accentuate that fact.&rdquo;&lt;/p&gt;&lt;p&gt;The undisputed queen of personalised prosthetics is Sophie de Oliveira Barata, who runs the Alternative Limb Project. Her studio has produced some of the most unique and eye-catching designs in the world, incorporating &lt;a href=\"http://www.nytimes.com/interactive/2013/11/03/magazine/03limbs_in.html\"&gt;scales, magnifying glasses, laser pointers, and ultra-realistic&lt;/a&gt; features such as freckles (see gallery). For the closing ceremony of the Paralympics, she crafted model Viktoria Modesta a baroque, jewelled leg decked with &lt;a href=\"http://www.thealternativelimbproject.com/#/crystallized-leg/4570775453\"&gt;crystals and mirrors&lt;/a&gt;. Even fashion designers are getting in the act &ndash; Alexander McQueen designer a pair of intricately carved &lt;a href=\"http://blog.metmuseum.org/alexandermcqueen/tag/no-13/\"&gt;wooden legs for Paralympic athlete Aimee Mullins&lt;/a&gt;. Made without any joints, there weren&rsquo;t very practical &ndash; but that&rsquo;s hardly an unusual criticism for catwalk creations.&lt;/p&gt;&lt;p&gt;No prosthetic comes cheap. Currently running blades demand a cool $4,000 apiece, while Metelka&rsquo;s most hi-tech leg &ndash; a motorised walking foot &ndash; is an eye-watering $90,000. This high cost is the major issue for those wanting to specialise their limbs, says Carter. &ldquo;If money wasn&rsquo;t an issue I&rsquo;d get all the legs I could have,&rdquo; he laughs. &ldquo;I&rsquo;d be the Imelda Marcos of prosthetics.&rdquo;&lt;/p&gt;&lt;p&gt;&lt;em&gt;If you would like to comment on this, or anything else you have seen on Future, head over to our &lt;/em&gt;&lt;a href=\"https://www.facebook.com/BBCFuture\"&gt;&lt;em&gt;Facebook&lt;/em&gt;&lt;/a&gt;&lt;em&gt; or &lt;/em&gt;&lt;a href=\"https://plus.google.com/u/0/b/107828172298602173375/107828172298602173375/posts\"&gt;&lt;em&gt;Google+&lt;/em&gt;&lt;/a&gt;&lt;em&gt; page, or message us on &lt;/em&gt;&lt;a href=\"https://twitter.com/BBC_Future\"&gt;&lt;em&gt;Twitter&lt;/em&gt;&lt;/a&gt;&lt;em&gt;.&lt;/em&gt;&lt;/p&gt;","BusinessUnit":"worldwide","CalloutBody":"","CalloutPosition":"","CalloutSubtitle":"","CalloutTitle":"","Campaign":null,"Collection":[],"DisableAdverts":false,"DisplayDate":"2014-01-23T00:00:00Z","HeadlineLong":"Prosthetics: Meet the man with 13 legs","HeadlineShort":"The man with 13 legs","HideRelated":false,"Intro":"From robotic legs to laser arms, the owners of artificial limbs are no longer content with only one replacement, as Frank Swain discovers.","IsSyndicated":false,"Location":null,"Partner":null,"PrimaryVertical":"wwfuture","Programme":null,"RelatedStory":[],"RelatedTag":null,"StoryType":"image","SummaryLong":"From robotic legs to laser arms, the owners of artificial limbs are no longer content with only one replacement","SummaryShort":"Amazing limbs that do things you cant","Tag":[]},"Metadata":{"CreationDateTime":"2014-11-18T09:14:24Z","Entity":"story","Guid":"237fe63a-faa0-4df9-80e8-03b4ff0066d5","Id":"wwfuture/story/20140123-the-man-with-13-legs","ModifiedDateTime":"2014-11-18T09:14:24Z","Project":"wwfuture","Slug":"20140123-the-man-with-13-legs"},"Urn":"urn:pubstack:jative:story:wwfuture/story/20140123-the-man-with-13-legs","_id":"559a56290b808738c4e2f685"},{"Content":{"AssetCustom":"","AssetIbroadcast":[],"AssetImage":[],"AssetImagePromo":null,"AssetInfographic":"","AssetInline":[],"AssetSelect":"gallery","AssetVideoIb2":null,"AssetVideoMps":null,"Author":[],"BodyHtml":"&lt;p&gt;A few years ago, I perched on the edge of my bed in a tiny flat, breathing in a cloud of acetone fumes, using a scalpel to pick at the corner of an electronic travel card. More than 10 million Londoners use these Oyster cards to ride the city&rsquo;s public transport network. I had decided to dissect mine. After letting the card sit in pink nail polish remover for a week, the plastic had softened enough that I could peel apart the layers. Buried inside was a tiny microchip attached to a fine copper wire: the radio frequency identification (RFID) chip.&lt;/p&gt;&lt;p&gt;My goal was to bury the chip under my skin, so that the machine barriers at the entrance to the Underground would fly open with a wave of my hand, as if I was some kind of technological wizard. But although I had the chip and an ex-Royal Marines medic willing to do the surgery, I failed to get my hands on the high-grade silicone I&rsquo;d need to coat the chip to prevent my body reacting against it. Since then, people have used the &lt;a href=\"https://www.youtube.com/watch?v=1BT6MU3gH80\"&gt;technique I helped popularise&lt;/a&gt; to put liberated Oyster chips in bracelets, rings, magic wands, even fruit, but the prize for first London transport cyborg is still up for grabs.&lt;/p&gt;&lt;p&gt;The person who does will find themselves inducted into the community of &ldquo;grinders&rdquo; &ndash; hobbyists who modify their own body with technological improvements.&nbsp; Just as you might find petrol heads poring over an engine, or hackers tinkering away at software code, grinders dream up ways to tweak their own bodies. One of the most popular upgrades is to implant a microchip under the skin, usually in the soft webbing between the thumb and forefinger.&lt;/p&gt;&lt;p&gt;{\"image\":{\"pid\":\"p01rx8sd\"}}&lt;/p&gt;&lt;p&gt;Take Amal Graafstra, a self-described &ldquo;adventure technologist&rdquo; and founder of biohacking company Dangerous Things in Seattle, Washington. He is a double implantee &ndash; he has a microchip in each hand.&lt;/p&gt;&lt;p&gt;In his right hand is a re-writable chip, the same kind used in Oyster travel cards, which can be used to store small amounts of data. By pressing his hand to his phone, information can be downloaded from his body or uploaded into it. The left contains a simple identity number that can be scanned to unlock his front door, log into his computer or even start a motorbike (see video, below).&lt;/p&gt;&lt;p&gt;{\"magnify\":{ \"id\": \"RFID-Enhanced-Motorcycle-Igniti\"}}&lt;/p&gt;&lt;p&gt;This month at the &lt;a href=\"http://brighterbrains.org/articles/entry/transhuman-visions-san-francisco-conference-tickets-on-sale-now\"&gt;Transhuman Visions&lt;/a&gt; conference in San Francisco, Graafstra set up an &ldquo;implantation station&rdquo; offering attendees the chance to be chipped at $50 a time. Using a large needle designed for microchipping pets, Graafstra injected a glass-coated RFID tag the size of a rice grain into each volunteer. By the end of the day Graafstra had created 15 new cyborgs.&lt;/p&gt;&lt;p&gt;For other people, though, the idea of implanting themselves with microchips may conjure up spectres of surveillance and totalitarian control. &ldquo;Every Hollywood movie has told them that implants are for tracking people,&rdquo; says Graafsta. &ldquo;People don&rsquo;t get that it's the same exact technology as the card in your wallet. When someone uses a credit card, wireless or not, they are tracked because several other corporations know who they are, when they purchased, how much they spent, and where they spent it.&rdquo;&lt;/p&gt;&lt;p&gt;Yet if that&rsquo;s true, what&rsquo;s the point of implanting it? Graafstra and his fellow cyborgs could just as easily use a chip inside plastic wallet to store data, and a key to open his front door or start a motorbike. &ldquo;Yes, basically you've taken an RFID access card normally stored in a pants pocket and moved it to a skin pocket,&rdquo; admits Graafstra. Still, there are some advantages: one benefit is that you&rsquo;ll never lose the chip, and it makes physical theft impossible &ndash; at least unless a thief is prepared for some gruesome surgery.&lt;/p&gt;&lt;p&gt;Graafsta also points out that embedding the chip under the skin reduces the distance that it can be read with a scanner, making it more secure.&nbsp; When it&rsquo;s in your arm or hand, there&rsquo;s less chance someone can surreptitiously scan your details, by sweeping a card reader nearby.&lt;/p&gt;&lt;p&gt;{\"image\":{\"pid\":\"p01rzyn0\"}}&lt;/p&gt;&lt;p&gt;Ultimately, implanted microchips offer a way to make your physical body machine-readable. Currently, there is no single standard of communicating with the machines that underpin society &ndash; from building access panels to ATMs &ndash; but an endless diversity of identification systems: magnetic strips, passwords, PIN numbers, security questions, and dongles. All of these are attempts to bridge the divide between your digital and physical identity, and if you forget or lose them, you are suddenly cut off from your bank account, your gym, your ride home, your proof of ID, and more. An implanted chip, by contrast, could act as our universal identity token for navigating the machine-regulated world.&lt;/p&gt;&lt;p&gt;Yet to work, such a chip would need to be truly universal and account for potential obsolescence. My own flirtation with implanted technology came to an end when I moved away from London, making an Oyster-equipped hand pointless. Even with a return to London on the cards, I&rsquo;m thinking twice about returning to my project, since Oyster cards are being phased out.&lt;/p&gt;&lt;p&gt;Such a development may actually be a cause for optimism for implant enthusiasts, however, because instead of Oyster cards, London's transport authority is allowing people to ride the subways and buses using bank cards. It marks the beginnings of a slow move toward a world where everything will be accessed from a single RFID microchip. If that day comes, I can&rsquo;t think of a safer place to keep it than inside my own body.&lt;/p&gt;&lt;p&gt;&lt;em&gt;If you would like to comment on this, or anything else you have seen on Future,
