primates with bare faces tend to have vision like ours (Thinkstock)","SynopsisMedium":"Other primates with bare faces tend to have vision like ours (Thinkstock)","SynopsisShort":"Other primates with bare faces tend to have vision like ours (Thinkstock)","TemplateUrl":"http://ichef.bbci.co.uk/wwfeatures/$recipe/images/live/p0/27/df/p027dfkh.jpg","Title":"organ_thinkstock.jpg"},"Metadata":{"CreationDateTime":"0001-01-01T00:00:00Z","Entity":"","Guid":"","Id":"p027dfkh","ModifiedDateTime":"0001-01-01T00:00:00Z","Project":"","Slug":""},"Urn":"urn:pubstack:jative:image:p027dfkh","_id":"559a55060b808738c4e2ccb3"}],"AssetSelect":"","AssetVideoIb2":null,"AssetVideoMps":null,"Author":[{"Content":{"AssetImage":[],"Description":"&lt;p&gt;Jason G Goldman is a\nscience writer based in Los Angeles. He writes about animal behavior, wildlife,\nand the complex relationship between humans and the rest of the animal kingdom.\nFind him on twitter: &lt;a href=\"http://twitter.com/jgold85\"&gt;@jgold85&lt;/a&gt;.&lt;/p&gt;","Email":[],"Links":null,"Name":"Jason G Goldman","PrimaryVertical":"wwfuture"},"Metadata":{"CreationDateTime":"2014-11-18T09:12:17Z","Entity":"author","Guid":"09cd12f4-0173-4bae-9a62-2613a216e20c","Id":"wwfuture/author/jason-goldman","ModifiedDateTime":"2014-11-18T09:12:17Z","Project":"wwfuture","Slug":"jason-goldman"},"Urn":"urn:pubstack:jative:author:wwfuture/author/jason-goldman","_id":"559a524d0b808738c4e25f79"}],"BodyHtml":"&lt;p&gt;Have you ever stopped to consider your face? Compared to most of the rest of the animal kingdom, the human face has at least one really peculiar feature: it's almost completely devoid of hair. Sure, some people grow beards or moustaches, but even a full pirate's beard would leave quite a bit of skin showing. They don't call us \"the hairless ape\" for nothing. How did we come to be so bare-faced?&lt;/p&gt;&lt;p&gt;The reasons we lost our body hair are still debated. Some researchers think that we lost our fur to &lt;a href=\"http://www.nytimes.com/2003/08/19/science/why-humans-and-their-fur-parted-ways.html\"&gt;free ourselves of parasites&lt;/a&gt;, such as lice. This might have made us more attractive to the opposite sex; bare skin would advertise our lack of parasites. Others have suggested that we lost most of our hair to &lt;a href=\"http://onlinelibrary.wiley.com/doi/10.1111/j.1469-7998.2007.00295.x/abstract?\"&gt;facilitate cooling&lt;/a&gt; as we moved from the shady forests to the hot savannah. Still others wonder whether nakedness is one of a number of juvenile traits that humans retain throughout their lifetimes; humans are thought to be, in one sense, juvenilised apes, who mature slower and live longer than our ape cousins.&lt;/p&gt;&lt;p&gt;Yet Mark Changizi, a neurobiologist at 2AI Labs in Boston, has an intriguing alternative explanation for why we don&rsquo;t have hair on our faces, at least when we compare ourselves to other primates. It's because we're walking, talking, breathing &lsquo;mood rings&rsquo;.&lt;/p&gt;&lt;p&gt;&lt;strong&gt;Colour reveal&lt;/strong&gt;&lt;/p&gt;&lt;p&gt;Mood rings were a short-lived fad of the mid 1970s. The idea behind wearing one was that it would act as a sort of emotional barometer, betraying your innermost feelings to anyone who took a glance at the jewellery. In truth, mood rings were little more than thermometers, designed to change colour according to body temperature. But the idea &ndash; that colour betrays emotion &ndash; isn't actually all that farfetched. The idea is pervasive in human culture. We become \"green with envy\". We turn red when angry or embarrassed. Sadness is referred to as \"the blues\". The truth may not be so far off.&lt;/p&gt;&lt;p&gt;{\"image\":{\"pid\":\"p027dfhn\"}}&lt;/p&gt;&lt;p&gt;Changizi, together with researchers Qiong Zhang and Shinsuke Shimojo, argues that our &lt;a href=\"http://www.ncbi.nlm.nih.gov/pmc/articles/PMC1618887/\"&gt;faces evolved their hairlessness to allow other members of our species the ability to read our emotions&lt;/a&gt;. Indeed, primate faces and &ndash; in some cases &ndash; rumps and genitalia change colours thanks to the underlying physiology of the skin. \"For highly social animals like most primates, one of the most important kinds of objects to be competent at perceiving and discriminating is other members of one's own species,\" he writes.&lt;/p&gt;&lt;p&gt;Most mammals, like dogs, horses, or bears, can only see blends of two colours when they look at the world. They're called dichromats, and they can only see yellows, blues, or the greens that form when blue and yellow mix &ndash; in addition to perceiving brightness. That's because their eyes have, in addition to the brightness-sensing rods, only two types of cones: ones sensitive to short or long wavelengths of light. But humans and some other primates are trichromats. We have a third type of cone, sensitive to medium wavelengths of light. Having that third cone means that we can also detect colours along a red-green continuum. (&lt;a href=\"http://www.bbc.com/future/story/20140905-the-women-with-super-human-vision\"&gt;A few lucky people are tetrachromats&lt;/a&gt;, which means they can see even more shades).&lt;/p&gt;&lt;p&gt;&lt;strong&gt;Blood flow&lt;/strong&gt;&lt;/p&gt;&lt;p&gt;But there's something odd about the three types of cones that trichromats have: they're not evenly spaced out. It just so happens that this odd assortment of cones allows our eyes to perceive properties of the blood circling through our bodies just beneath the skin: how saturated with oxygen the haemoglobin is, and how concentrated the haemoglobin is. Changes in those two variables result in predictable changes in skin colouration.&lt;/p&gt;&lt;p&gt;It's actually quite remarkable how many colours human skin can be. We're used to thinking of skin as being white, black, or brown. But those are just a baseline. Darker faces still blush, after all, something that even Darwin noticed. Skin appears redder as haemoglobin becomes more and more oxygenated. Reductions in oxygen saturation turn the skin green (which is just how veins carrying de-oxygenated blood back to the heart look). On the other hand, a greater accumulation of blood in a certain area turns the skin somewhat blue. Just like a bruise. And a reduction in blood concentration does the opposite, making the skin appear yellow. Which, indeed, is how we describe somebody who isn't looking very healthy. \"Colour is sufficiently suggestive of emotion,\" writes Changizi, \"that cartoons often use colour on a face to indicate emotional state.\" And it's something that even children can recognise effortlessly.&lt;/p&gt;&lt;p&gt;{\"image\":{\"pid\":\"p027dfkh\"}}&lt;/p&gt;&lt;p&gt;Dichromats &ndash; the dogs, bunnies, and bears of the world, along with certain primates &ndash; can only perceive changes along the blue-yellow dimension, allowing them to notice changes only in blood concentration. In some ways, that's a useful skill. It's probably best to avoid someone who looks yellow, to avoid becoming infected with whatever disease or sickness they're trying to fight off, or to escape being near someone who might imminently vomit. On the other hand, a bruise betrays injury, someone who will be easier to beat in a fight over resources.&lt;/p&gt;&lt;p&gt;Here's the best part: if it's true that facial bare skin evolved to allow for colour signalling, then other trichromatic primates should have visible skin too. To see if his hypothesis held up, Changizi looked at 97 different primate species. Sure enough, he found that monochromatic and dichromatic primates are furry, while those, including us, who have three types of cones have more visible facial skin.&lt;/p&gt;&lt;p&gt;Even if bare skin was initially selected for something other than colour signalling, it is likely the case that both colour vision and visible skin became evolutionarily entangled, at least among primates, argues Changizi.&lt;/p&gt;&lt;p&gt;So next time your friends ask you what you're feeling, just tell them to look at your skin. They should be able to figure it out: after all, they have millions of years of evolution on their side.&lt;/p&gt;&lt;p&gt;&lt;em&gt;If you would like to comment on this article, or anything else you have seen on Future, head over to our &lt;/em&gt;&lt;a href=\"https://www.facebook.com/BBCFuture\"&gt;&lt;em&gt;Facebook&lt;/em&gt;&lt;/a&gt;&lt;em&gt; or &lt;/em&gt;&lt;a href=\"https://plus.google.com/u/0/b/107828172298602173375/107828172298602173375/posts\"&gt;&lt;em&gt;Google+&lt;/em&gt;&lt;/a&gt;&lt;em&gt; page, or message us on &lt;/em&gt;&lt;a href=\"https://twitter.com/BBC_Future\"&gt;&lt;em&gt;Twitter&lt;/em&gt;&lt;/a&gt;.&lt;/p&gt;","BusinessUnit":"worldwide","CalloutBody":"","CalloutPosition":"","CalloutSubtitle":"","CalloutTitle":"","Campaign":null,"Collection":[{"Content":{"AssetImage":[],"Campaign":null,"CollectionOverrides":null,"CollectionType":"column","Description":"The mysteries of our bodies. Jason G Goldman investigates why our body parts  from eyes to hands  ended up the way they did, and how they made us human.","Name":"Body Matters","Partner":null,"PrimaryVertical":"wwfuture","Title":"Body Matters","CreationDateTime":"2015-05-20T11:42:22.339015Z","Entity":"collection","Guid":"fa461851-f43f-466f-9051-c20a0acdc6cc","Id":"wwfuture/column/body-matters","ModifiedDateTime":"2015-05-20T11:42:22.339015Z","Project":"wwfuture","Slug":"column/body-matters"},"Metadata":{"CreationDateTime":"2015-05-20T11:42:22.339015Z","Entity":"collection","Guid":"fa461851-f43f-466f-9051-c20a0acdc6cc","Id":"wwfuture/column/body-matters","ModifiedDateTime":"2015-05-20T11:42:22.339015Z","Project":"wwfuture","Slug":"column/body-matters"},"Urn":"urn:pubstack:jative:collection:wwfuture/column/body-matters","_id":"559a52610b808738c4e26299"},{"Content":{"AssetImage":[],"Campaign":null,"CollectionOverrides":[],"CollectionType":"section","Description":"Health","Name":"Health","Partner":null,"PrimaryVertical":"wwfuture","Title":"Health"},"Metadata":{"CreationDateTime":"2015-05-20T10:44:06.503434Z","Entity":"collection","Guid":"00a9a33a-e393-4d09-8029-7e9d5bd1d8cb","Id":"wwfuture/section/health","ModifiedDateTime":"2015-05-20T13:52:37.074442Z","Project":"wwfuture","Slug":"section/health"},"Urn":"urn:pubstack:jative:collection:wwfuture/section/health","_id":"559a52620b808738c4e262db"},{"Content":{"AssetImage":[],"Campaign":null,"CollectionOverrides":[],"CollectionType":"section","Description":"Science &amp; Environment","Name":"Science &amp; Environment","Partner":null,"PrimaryVertical":"wwfuture","Title":"Science &amp; Environment"},"Metadata":{"CreationDateTime":"2015-05-20T10:51:45.984509Z","Entity":"collection","Guid":"1aa4ccec-87b5-44b8-b5af-c553debaf6f6","Id":"wwfuture/section/science-environment","ModifiedDateTime":"2015-05-20T13:51:20.083392Z","Project":"wwfuture","Slug":"section/science-environment"},"Urn":"urn:pubstack:jative:collection:wwfuture/section/science-environment","_id":"559a52620b808738c4e262dc"}],"DisableAdverts":false,"DisplayDate":"2014-09-26T00:00:00Z","HeadlineLong":"Evolution: Why dont we have hairier faces?","HeadlineShort":"Are hairy faces less evolved?","HideRelated":false,"Intro":"Men may have beards, but compared to many of our primate cousins our faces are pretty hairless  why is that? Jason G Goldman explores the enigma of our bare-faced cheeks.","IsSyndicated":true,"Location":null,"Partner":null,"PrimaryVertical":"wwfuture","Programme":null,"RelatedStory":[{"Content":{"AssetCustom":"","AssetIbroadcast":null,"AssetImage":[],"AssetImagePromo":null,"AssetInfographic":"","AssetInline":[],"AssetSelect":"","AssetVideoIb2":null,"AssetVideoMps":null,"Author":[],"BodyHtml":"&lt;p&gt;Fran Fulton is 66, and she&rsquo;s been fully blind for about 10 years. A few weeks ago, all that changed.&lt;/p&gt;&lt;p&gt;Fulton suffers from retinitis pigmentosa &ndash; a degenerative eye disease that slowly causes light-sensitive cells in the retina to die off. Over the course of several years she lost her sight, and for the past 10 years she hasn&rsquo;t been able to see anything at all. But in late July, Fulton was outfitted with a system called the Argus II. A pair of camera-equipped glasses are hooked up to electrodes implanted in her eyeball, which feed her brain visual information. Using the system, she can now see the world again. What&rsquo;s the experience like?&lt;/p&gt;&lt;blockquote&gt;&lt;p&gt;&ldquo;When they &lsquo;turned me on&rsquo; so to speak it was absolutely the most breathtaking experience&rdquo;&lt;/p&gt;&lt;/blockquote&gt;&lt;p&gt;&ldquo;When they &lsquo;turned me on&rsquo; so to speak it was absolutely the most breathtaking experience,&rdquo; she says. &ldquo;I was just so overwhelmed and so excited, my heart started beating so fast I had to put my hand on my chest because I thought it was going to pop.&rdquo;&lt;/p&gt;&lt;p&gt;As both cameras and our understanding of the visual system improve, new techniques to restore sight to the blind are progressing too. Devices like the Argus II are able to bypass damaged eyes to restore some vision to those who have lost it. It&rsquo;s not the same as fully restored vision, and it&rsquo;s still early days &ndash; there are only six people in the US with the Argus II &ndash; but researchers hope that as they learn more about vision they can help those who&rsquo;ve lost it get it back.&lt;/p&gt;&lt;p&gt;{\"image\":{\"pid\":\"p0277gdn\"}}&lt;/p&gt;&lt;p&gt;The Argus II system is made up of three parts: a pair of glasses, a converter box, and an electrode array. The glasses aren&rsquo;t corrective, they are simply a vehicle for the camera &ndash; and that camera is no more complicated than the versions found in modern smart phones. The image from the camera is then transmitted down into a converter box that can be carried in a purse or pocket. This box sends signals to the electrode array implanted onto the patient&rsquo;s retina. Essentially, what the Argus II does is skip over the cells that retinitis pigmentosa has killed to get visual signals to the brain.&lt;/p&gt;&lt;p&gt;Robert Greenberg, the president and CEO of Second Sight, the company that developed Argus II, explains that the eye is like a multi-layer cake. On one layer are the light-sensitive cells, called &ldquo;rods&rdquo; and &ldquo;cones&rdquo;, that sighted people rely on to take in light and turn that into visual information. But for those with retinitis pigmentosa, those cells are dead. &ldquo;We&rsquo;re bypassing those dead cells and going to the next layer of the cake,&rdquo; Greenberg explains.&lt;/p&gt;&lt;p&gt;This means that Argus II has to convert the information from the camera into signals that the electrodes implanted in the eye can use, and that the brain can interpret. Figuring out how to achieve that was the focus of Greenberg&rsquo;s PhD thesis. But there was a bigger hurdle to come, he says: working out a way to implant electrodes onto the paper-thin retina inside the eye.&lt;/p&gt;&lt;p&gt;{\"image\":{\"pid\":\"p0277hkn\"}}&lt;/p&gt;&lt;p&gt;&ldquo;The retina is like one-ply toilet paper,&rdquo; he says. &ldquo;Developing something that can sit on the surface of the retina without damaging it is really difficult. That was tougher than figuring out the algorithms.&rdquo;&lt;/p&gt;&lt;p&gt;For patients, though, the whole thing is remarkably simple. The surgery to implant the electrodes takes just a few hours and patients go home the same day with an implant that wraps around one of their eyes and is secured by a tiny tack the size of a human hair. After about a week to heal, the patient returns to get the glasses, to have their new electrodes tuned, and to train them on how to use the system. On the converter box there are knobs that let users increase or decrease things like the brightness and contrast. Then they go home with their new pair of eyes.&lt;/p&gt;&lt;p&gt;&lt;strong&gt;Sight beyond sight&lt;/strong&gt;&lt;/p&gt;&lt;p&gt;So what do people using Argus II actually see? Greenberg says it&rsquo;s best imagined as looking like a pixelated image, or staring at a digital scoreboard held just in front of your eyes. There are regions of light and dark that collectively the brain recognises as an image.&lt;/p&gt;&lt;p&gt;Fulton, however, says it&rsquo;s difficult to describe exactly what she sees. &ldquo;People say you&rsquo;ll see shapes,&rdquo; she says. &ldquo;Well yeah but it&rsquo;s the electrical impulses, and it&rsquo;s about learning how to interpret them. It&rsquo;s not that it&rsquo;s hard, it&rsquo;s just a learning curve. It&rsquo;s something that I&rsquo;m learning.&rdquo;&lt;/p&gt;&lt;p&gt;{\"image\":{\"pid\":\"p0277h43\"}}&lt;/p&gt;&lt;p&gt;Fulton says that mostly what she can see is areas of light and dark. She recently had dinner with friends. When they were leaving the restaurant she was able to hone in on a person&rsquo;s light shirt. &ldquo;I didn&rsquo;t need to use a sighted guide, they were in front of me and I just followed them out,&rdquo; she says. Other patients report that things like fireworks and Christmas trees are especially visible. &ldquo;I can&rsquo;t wait for something to happen that I can go to fireworks, I haven&rsquo;t seen fireworks in a very long time and I am looking forward to that,&rdquo; Fran says.&lt;/p&gt;&lt;p&gt;Many patients, Fulton included, continue doing vision therapy to improve their sight and train their brain to better interpret the signals.&lt;/p&gt;&lt;p&gt;For Fulton, who works as a disability advocate, getting around now is so much easier. &ldquo;The first time I left work with it &ndash; I work on the third floor and there are three elevators, and I heard the bell to go down. I lined myself up in front of the door and I walked straight in. I didn&rsquo;t bump my left shoulder, didn&rsquo;t bump my right shoulder, I didn&rsquo;t have to use my cane to check. Every day it&rsquo;s very exciting, and never in my lifetime did I ever think something like this could happen.&rdquo;&lt;/p&gt;&lt;p&gt;Fulton has long used a cane to detect obstacles in her way, but now her awareness of her surroundings is much more detailed. &ldquo;I am able to now identify doorways and objects on the street. I can&rsquo;t tell you whether it&rsquo;s a flowerpot or a homeless person collecting money, but I can tell you there&rsquo;s an object there.&rdquo;&lt;/p&gt;&lt;p&gt;&lt;strong&gt;Defining tech&lt;/strong&gt;&lt;/p&gt;&lt;p&gt;Argus II isn&rsquo;t perfect: it&rsquo;s only black and white, for starters. And it&rsquo;s not like you&rsquo;re feeding a full image to the brain: users can&rsquo;t read signs, or recognise faces, or identify objects &ndash; at least, not usually. &ldquo;I&rsquo;ve been quite successful at identifying a triangle versus a circle and a square,&rdquo; Fran boasts.&lt;/p&gt;&lt;p&gt;It&rsquo;s also important to note that this is not a system that all blind people can use &ndash; they have to have an intact retina for the implant to work. Those who lost their site to things like diabetes, glaucoma or infection and who have damage to the retina can&rsquo;t use the Argus II system.&lt;/p&gt;&lt;p&gt;Greenberg says Second Sight is working on a new implant that bypasses even the retinal layer, and implants electrodes directly onto the visual region of the brain.&lt;/p&gt;&lt;p&gt;But for those who have been blind for years, simply seeing shapes again is pretty exciting. &ldquo;I&rsquo;m very much looking forward to being able to see my grandchildren,&rdquo; Fulton says. &ldquo;I won&rsquo;t be able to see their faces, but I know they&rsquo;ll have great fun standing in a room and say &lsquo;grandma find me!&rsquo; and I&rsquo;ll be able to tell the difference between the four-year-old and the seven-year-old.&rdquo;&lt;/p&gt;&lt;p&gt;&lt;em&gt;If you would like to comment on this article, or anything else you have seen on Future, head over to our &lt;/em&gt;&lt;a href=\"https://www.facebook.com/BBCFuture\"&gt;&lt;em&gt;Facebook&lt;/em&gt;&lt;/a&gt;&lt;em&gt; or &lt;/em&gt;&lt;a href=\"https://plus.google.com/u/0/b/107828172298602173375/107828172298602173375/posts\"&gt;&lt;em&gt;Google+&lt;/em&gt;&lt;/a&gt;&lt;em&gt; page, or message us on &lt;/em&gt;&lt;a href=\"https://twitter.com/BBC_Future\"&gt;&lt;em&gt;Twitter&lt;/em&gt;&lt;/a&gt;.&lt;/p&gt;","BusinessUnit":"worldwide","CalloutBody":"","CalloutPosition":"","CalloutSubtitle":"","CalloutTitle":"","Campaign":null,"Collection":[],"DisableAdverts":false,"DisplayDate":"2014-09-23T00:00:00Z","HeadlineLong":"I was blind now I have bionic eyes","HeadlineShort":"Im blind but I have bionic eyes","HideRelated":false,"Intro":"What is it like to see again after years of blindness? Rose Eveleth asks a woman whose sight was restored with a remarkable new eye implant.","IsSyndicated":true,"Location":null,"Partner":null,"PrimaryVertical":"wwfuture","Programme":null,"RelatedStory":[],"RelatedTag":null,"StoryType":"image","SummaryLong":"What is it like to see again after years of blindness? Rose Eveleth asks a woman whose sight was restored with a remarkable new eye implant.","SummaryShort":"How one woman regained her sight ","Tag":[]},"Metadata":{"CreationDateTime":"2014-11-18T09:14:24Z","Entity":"story","Guid":"92415001-bf74-4e82-8048-b53e306fae30","Id":"wwfuture/story/20140923-im-blind-but-i-have-bionic-eyes","ModifiedDateTime":"2014-11-18T09:14:24Z","Project":"wwfuture","Slug":"20140923-im-blind-but-i-have-bionic-eyes"},"Urn":"urn:pubstack:jative:story:wwfuture/story/20140923-im-blind-but-i-have-bionic-eyes","_id":"559a563f0b808738c4e2f959"},{"Content":{"AssetCustom":"","AssetIbroadcast":null,"AssetImage":[],"AssetImagePromo":null,"AssetInfographic":"","AssetInline":[],"AssetSelect":"","AssetVideoIb2":null,"AssetVideoMps":null,"Author":[],"BodyHtml":"&lt;p&gt;&lt;img src=\"http://www.bbc.com/future/bespoke/life_in_colour/img/4594_Life_in_Colour_140827_header.jpg\" width=&ldquo;464&rdquo; height=&ldquo;60&rdquo;&gt;&lt;/p&gt;&lt;p&gt;As Concetta Antico took her pupils to the park for an art lesson, she would often question them about the many shades she saw flashing before her eyes. &ldquo;I&rsquo;d say, &lsquo;Look at the light on the water &ndash; can you see the pink shimmering across that rock? Can you see the red on the edge of that leaf there?&rsquo;&rdquo; The students would all nod in agreement. It was only years later that she realised they were just too polite to tell the truth: the colours she saw so vividly were invisible to them.&lt;/p&gt;&lt;p&gt;Today, she knows that this is a symptom of a condition known as &ldquo;tetrachromacy&rdquo;. Thanks to a variation in a gene that influences the development of their retinas, people like Antico can see colours invisible to most of us. Consider a pebble pathway. What appears dull grey to you or me shines like a jeweller&rsquo;s display to Antico. &ldquo;The little stones jump out at me with oranges, yellows, greens, blues and pinks,&rdquo; she says. &ldquo;I&rsquo;m kind of shocked when I realise what other people aren&rsquo;t seeing.&rdquo;&lt;/p&gt;&lt;p&gt;Tetrachromats are rare enough, but Antico is particularly remarkable, since, as an artist, she is able to give us a rare view into that world. &ldquo;Her artwork might tap into a structure that all of us can appreciate,&rdquo; says Kimberly Jameson at the University of California, Irvine, &lt;a href=\"http://www.tenthousandthings.info\"&gt;who has studied Antico extensively&lt;/a&gt;. It&rsquo;s even possible that she might suggest ways for more people to see the same way.&lt;/p&gt;&lt;p&gt;{\"image\":{\"pid\":\"p0267qts\"}}&lt;/p&gt;&lt;p&gt;The question of &lt;a href=\"http://www.bbc.com/future/story/20120209-do-we-all-see-the-same-colours\"&gt;whether we all see the same colours&lt;/a&gt; has a long history in philosophy and science. In the past, there seemed little reason to expect huge differences. We know that almost everyone has three types of &ldquo;cone cells&rdquo; in their retina that each respond to a different bandwidth of light. The colour of an object depends on the particular combination of those signals, but although the exact sensitivity may vary between people, overall one person&rsquo;s colours should roughly match another person&rsquo;s. The exceptions were thought to be colour-blind people, where one of the cones is faulty. With reduced sensitivity at certain wavelengths, they struggle to tell the difference between reds and greens, for instance.&nbsp;&lt;/p&gt;&lt;p&gt;In theory, though, it could go the other way: according to some estimates, an extra cone would offer a hundred different variants to each colour that humans normally see. We know that this happens in nature: zebrafinches and goldfish both have a fourth cone that seems to help them differentiate apparently identical colours. About 20 years ago Gabriele Jordan at the University of Newcastle and John Mollon at the University of Cambridge proposed a way that it might be possible in humans too.&lt;/p&gt;&lt;p&gt;The crux of Jordan&rsquo;s argument lay in the fact that the gene for our red and green cone types lies on the X chromosome. Since women have two X chromosomes, they could potentially carry two different versions of the gene, each encoding for a cone that is sensitive to slightly different parts of the spectrum. In addition to the other two, unaffected cones, they would therefore have four in total &ndash; making them a &ldquo;tetrachromat&rdquo;. For these reasons, it&rsquo;s thought to be a condition exclusive to women, though researchers can&rsquo;t totally rule out the possibility that men may somehow inherit it too.&lt;/p&gt;&lt;p&gt;{\"image\":{\"pid\":\"p0267psg\"}}&lt;/p&gt;&lt;p&gt;Proving that these people actually see the world differently has involved a two-decade journey, however. Although the relevant combination of genes does not seem to be especially rare &ndash; perhaps 12% of women might have four distinct cones &ndash; many of the people that Jordan tested just didn&rsquo;t seem to show any differences in their perception. But by 2010, she had found a subject who perfectly acted the part of a tetrachromat. Jordan&rsquo;s &ldquo;acid test&rdquo; involved coloured discs showing different mixtures of pigment, such as a green made of yellow and blue. The mixtures were too subtle for most people to notice: almost all people would see the same shade of olive green, but each combination should give out a subtly different spectrum of light that would be perceptible to someone with a fourth cone. Sure enough, Jordan&rsquo;s &lt;a href=\"http://www.journalofvision.org/content/10/8/12.full\"&gt;subject was able to differentiate between the different mixtures each time&lt;/a&gt;. &ldquo;When you ask them to discriminate between the two mixtures, a tetrachromat can do it very quickly. They don&rsquo;t hesitate,&rdquo; says Jordan.&lt;/p&gt;&lt;p&gt;{\"image\":{\"pid\":\"p0267pvz\"}}&lt;/p&gt;&lt;p&gt;But what do those colours actually look like? Unfortunately, Jordan&rsquo;s much-prized subject has not been available for media interviews. But once the abilities of the woman &ldquo;&lt;a href=\"http://discovermagazine.com/2012/jul-aug/06-humans-with-super-human-vision\"&gt;with super-human vision&lt;/a&gt;&rdquo; became known, many more potential tetrachromats have come forward who might be able to give us an insight.&lt;/p&gt;&lt;p&gt;One of those was &lt;a href=\"http://www.psychologytoday.com/blog/tasting-the-universe/201310/dna-results-positive-tetrachromacy-0\"&gt;Maureen Seaberg&lt;/a&gt;, a journalist and author in New York who took the genetics test after hearing &lt;a href=\"http://www.radiolab.org/story/211119-colors/\"&gt;Radiolab&rsquo;s podcast on the subject&lt;/a&gt;. &ldquo;I have always had polite disagreements with people about shades of colours,&rdquo; she says. When clothes shopping for instance, she often finds that apparently matching tops and skirts seem to be a different shade to her, clashing horribly &ndash; even though no one else seems to notice it. Her sensitivity can sometimes be baffling to those around: when helping to restore a house, she once rejected 32 paint samples before settling on the right shade. &ldquo;The beiges were too yellow and not blue enough, not cool enough; some of the almonds were too orangey,&rdquo; she says &ndash; distinctions that were much to the confusion of her building contractor. Of course, that is only anecdotal evidence, but it perhaps tells us a little about the way that apparently identical shades could appear strikingly different to a tetrachromat.&lt;/p&gt;&lt;p&gt;Antico has a similar story. She says she has always known that her eyes saw the world differently to other people &ndash; a talent that was soon noticed by her family. &ldquo;When I was a very little girl, my mother looked at me and said &lsquo;You are going to be an artist and art instructor&rsquo;.&rdquo; Today, Antico has fulfilled that prediction, with &lt;a href=\"http://concettaantico.com/\"&gt;her own gallery in San Diego, California&lt;/a&gt;, where she uses her enhanced vision to create vibrant paintings bursting with colour. Consider the following painting of a rainbow eucalyptus. &ldquo;The tubes of paint were flying. The yellows, the violets, the lime greens &ndash; I was ferociously mixing on the palette trying to produce all the streams of colour in the bark,&rdquo; says Antico. When compared to the real thing, the resulting canvas suggests that Antico is seeing more than the average eye.&lt;/p&gt;&lt;p&gt;{\"image\":{\"pid\":\"p0267p60\"}}&lt;/p&gt;&lt;p&gt;It was when looking at one of those paintings one day that a customer suggested that Antico contact some researchers who work on tetrachromacy. The genetics test came back positive, and she began collaborating on a series of experiments with Jameson and her colleagues, including Alissa Winkler at the University of Nevada, Reno.&lt;/p&gt;&lt;p&gt;Jameson immediately suspected the Antico&rsquo;s genes might also give her enhanced vision in dim lighting. &ldquo;If you look at her pictures of dawn, she paints a lot of colours and renders them in very low lighting,&rdquo; she says &ndash; conditions that normally reduce our vision to greyscale. That could just be creative licence, but Antico claims she is actually seeing the colours there in front of her. Sure enough, Jameson&rsquo;s experiments have shown that the luminance of different colours is indeed altered for Antico, making them pop out in the half light.&lt;/p&gt;&lt;p&gt;{\"image\":{\"pid\":\"p0267p9k\"}}&lt;/p&gt;&lt;p&gt;Enhanced sensitivity is not always a blessing, though. &ldquo;The grocery store is a nightmare,&rdquo; she says. &ldquo;It&rsquo;s like a trash pile of colour coming in at every angle.&rdquo; That enhanced sensitivity might explain why she finds solace in plain white surfaces. &ldquo;People find that extraordinary that white is my favourite colour, but it makes sense because it is so peaceful and restful for my eyes. There is still a lot of colour in it, but it&rsquo;s not hurting me.&rdquo;&lt;/p&gt;&lt;p&gt;Not all tetrachromats have striking abilities. Jameson has found that Antico&rsquo;s perception outstrips other tetrochromats who have not trained artistically. &ldquo;Concetta is the perfect storm for tetrachromacy because she has a huge amount of perceptual learning experience by working with colour on a daily basis.&rdquo; If that is confirmed with further research, Antico hopes that she might be able to develop a training system to help all tetrachromatic children realise their potential.&lt;/p&gt;&lt;p&gt;Antico&rsquo;s ultimate ambitions are even greater: to help us all see the world a little differently. Anecdotally, she says that a few of her students have started to notice some of the extra shades for themselves. &ldquo;It&rsquo;s as if a curtain is being lifted.&rdquo; Of course, without the genes, we can never achieve full tetrachromatic vision &ndash; but perhaps people like Antico can point out some differences that are just perceptible to our eyes, with training.&lt;/p&gt;&lt;p&gt;This is a particularly poignant issue for Antico. Thanks to the random draw of the genetic lottery, the particular gene variant that gave rise to her amazing vision has meant that her own daughter is colour blind. Perhaps one day, further research could suggest new ways to help everyone &ndash; including her daughter &ndash; to make the most of their colour vision, however limited. &ldquo;What if we tetrachromats can show the way to colour for people who are less fortunate than us?&rdquo; she says. &ldquo;I want everyone to realise how beautiful the world is.&rdquo;&lt;/p&gt;&lt;p&gt;&lt;em&gt;Do you see colours differently? If you would like to comment on this article, or anything else you have seen on Future, head over to our &lt;/em&gt;&lt;a href=\"https://www.facebook.com/BBCFuture\"&gt;&lt;em&gt;Facebook&lt;/em&gt;&lt;/a&gt;&lt;em&gt; or &lt;/em&gt;&lt;a href=\"https://plus.google.com/u/0/b/107828172298602173375/107828172298602173375/posts\"&gt;&lt;em&gt;Google+&lt;/em&gt;&lt;/a&gt;&lt;em&gt; page, or message us on &lt;/em&gt;&lt;a href=\"https://twitter.com/BBC_Future\"&gt;&lt;em&gt;Twitter&lt;/em&gt;&lt;/a&gt;.&lt;/p&gt;&lt;p&gt;To read more about tetrachromacy research and Concetta Antico, visit: &lt;a href=\"http://www.tenthousandthings.info\"&gt;www.tenthousandthings.info&lt;/a&gt; and &lt;a href=\"http://www.concettaantico.com\"&gt;www.concettaantico.com&lt;/a&gt;.&lt;/p&gt;&lt;p style=\"position: relative; display: block; width:100%; padding-bottom:110%\"&gt;&lt;iframe src=\"http://www.bbc.com/future/bespoke/life_in_colour/\" frameborder=\"0\" scrolling=\"no\" style=\"position:absolute;width:100%;height:100%;top:0;left:0;\"&gt;&lt;/iframe&gt;&lt;p&gt;&lt;span&gt;To read more about tetrachromacy research and &lt;span&gt;Concetta Antico&lt;/span&gt;, visit: &lt;a href=\"http://www.tenthousandthings.info\"&gt;www.tenthousandthings.info&lt;/a&gt; &lt;span&gt;and&lt;em&gt; &lt;span&gt;&nbsp;&lt;/span&gt;&lt;/em&gt;&lt;a href=\"http://www.concettaantico.com\"&gt;www.concettaantico.com&lt;/a&gt;. &lt;/span&gt;&lt;/span&gt;&lt;/p&gt;","BusinessUnit":"worldwide","CalloutBody":"","CalloutPosition":"","CalloutSubtitle":"","CalloutTitle":"","Campaign":null,"Collection":[],"DisableAdverts":false,"DisplayDate":"2014-09-05T00:00:00Z","HeadlineLong":"The women with superhuman vision","HeadlineShort":"The women with superhuman vision","HideRelated":false,"Intro":"A tiny group of people can see invisible colours that no-one else can perceive, discovers David Robson. How do they do it?","IsSyndicated":true,"Location":null,"Partner":null,"PrimaryVertical":"wwfuture","Programme":null,"RelatedStory":[],"RelatedTag":null,"StoryType":"image","SummaryLong":"A tiny group of people can see invisible colours that no-one else can perceive, discovers David Robson. How do they do it?","SummaryShort":"Theyve seen a world no-one else can view","Tag":[]},"Metadata":{"CreationDateTime":"2014-11-18T09:16:19Z","Entity":"story","Guid":"72d071f5-03f3-4238-b87c-74c4a9e13d5d","Id":"wwfuture/story/20140905-the-women-with-super-human-vision","ModifiedDateTime":"2014-11-18T09:16:19Z","Project":"wwfuture","Slug":"20140905-the-women-with-super-human-vision"},"Urn":"urn:pubstack:jative:story:wwfuture/story/20140905-the-women-with-super-human-vision","_id":"559a563d0b808738c4e2f933"},{"Content":{"AssetCustom":"","AssetIbroadcast":null,"AssetImage":[],"AssetImagePromo":null,"AssetInfographic":"","AssetInline":[],"AssetSelect":"","AssetVideoIb2":null,"AssetVideoMps":null,"Author":[],"BodyHtml":"&lt;p&gt;&lt;img src=\"http://www.bbc.com/future/bespoke/life_in_colour/img/4594_Life_in_Colour_140827_header.jpg\" width=&ldquo;464&rdquo; height=&ldquo;60&rdquo;&gt;&lt;/p&gt;&lt;p&gt;We can never know what was going through our ancestors&rsquo; minds, tens of thousands of years ago, when they first picked up natural crayons and began painting their bodies. But it is perhaps significant that they chose a rich, red ochre &ndash; the colour of our blood and a vivid reminder of life, and death.&nbsp;&lt;/p&gt;&lt;p&gt;Today, shades of scarlet are linked with power, aggression, and sex &ndash; from the vermilion of the British Queen&rsquo;s royal regalia to the gaudy neon of Amsterdam&rsquo;s red&ndash;light district. And those associations may not be coincidence. A new branch of science called &ldquo;colour psychology&rdquo; has found that red can have a profound influence on our mood, perceptions and actions. Wearing red can even change your physiology and balance of hormones and alter your performance in a football match. So what is it about the shades of ruby, crimson, and scarlet that makes them so potent?&lt;/p&gt;&lt;p&gt;&lt;strong&gt;Warning sign&lt;/strong&gt;&lt;/p&gt;&lt;p&gt;There is no doubt that our perception of red coincides with one of the most important events in our evolutionary history. Many mammals, like dogs, fail to differentiate between red and green. But as our early primate ancestors were adapting to life in the jungle, they evolved a new kind of cell in their retina that allowed them to pick out the bright, red fruit from the foliage. That enhanced perception would then lend itself to new forms of social signalling. Red skin &ndash; caused by blood pumping near the surface of the skin &ndash; is an important sign of dominance for many primates. Mandrill monkeys are perhaps the most famous example, with vivid markings on their face and bottom that signal their position in the group&rsquo;s strict hierarchy; the fitter, and more dominant an individual is, the redder he appears. Reading their competitors&rsquo; fitness therefore prevents the lower ranking monkeys to take on a fight they are sure to lose.&lt;/p&gt;&lt;p&gt;{\"image\":{\"pid\":\"p025p2p2\"}}&lt;/p&gt;&lt;p&gt;It was only in 2004 that two psychologists &ndash; Russell Hill and Robert Barton at the University of Durham &ndash; began to wonder whether humans might react in a similar way. Although we don&rsquo;t tend to flush a vivid, inflamed red like a mandrill, we do sometimes burn up with anger. So the sight of red clothes could perhaps carry associations of aggression and dominance. Hill and Barton were stuck for ways to investigate the idea, however, until the 2004 Olympics gave them the perfect opportunity. In combat sports like boxing and tae kwon do, the athletes were randomly assigned either red or blue kits, allowing the scientists to compare the same athletes&rsquo; performances when they were wearing different colours.&lt;/p&gt;&lt;p&gt;{\"image\":{\"pid\":\"p025p5ll\"}}&lt;/p&gt;&lt;p&gt;Tracking their progress throughout the games, Hill found that those assigned the red kits were about 5% more likely to win their bout than the blues. &ldquo;Simply wearing red doesn&rsquo;t turn you into an excellent competitor,&rdquo; says Hill. &ldquo;But it helps tip the balance between winning and losing when people are fairly evenly matched.&rdquo; This first &ldquo;study in scarlet&rdquo; triggered a host of other experiments, finding similar results with football penalty shooters, for instance: they are less likely to score if the goalie is wearing red. &nbsp;Soon, colour psychology was a credible scientific field in its own right. &ldquo;That paper was really responsible for this resurgence in interest in colour and its possible effects,&rdquo; says Andrew Elliot at the University of Rochester in New York State.&lt;/p&gt;&lt;p&gt;The exact reason for these effects still remains a matter of debate. Elliot points to studies showing that people who wear red feel more dominant themselves, triggering an increased heart rate and testosterone boost that could improve their performance. Or the red might intimidate the competitor &ndash; in the same way that less dominant mandrills may avoid approaching their leaders with crimson faces. &ldquo;If you see red you&rsquo;ll feel fear and your lower status, and your testosterone will drop,&rdquo; says Elliot. Alternatively, it might have been down to the referees; one German team manipulated videos to change the kits of tae kwon do players before showing them to professional referees. &ldquo;Simply changing colours changed the way experienced referees were scoring bouts,&rdquo; Hill says &ndash; whoever was wearing red won the referee&rsquo;s favour.&lt;/p&gt;&lt;p&gt;Away from the sports hall, similar thought processes could lead to your downfall in a casino. &lt;a href=\"http://yadda.icm.edu.pl/yadda/element/bwmeta1.element.elsevier-08c502e5-9058-3ec5-99d3-e707ee05d495\"&gt;Playing with red poker chips seem to make people bet more than those using blue or white tiles&lt;/a&gt;, perhaps because they seem like the chips of winners. On the plus side, red clothes might also help you to perform better in an interview. Some fashion experts suggest &lt;a href=\"http://www.bbc.com/capital/story/20140827-the-psychology-of-tie-colours\"&gt;red ties project authority and dominance in the workplace, as BBC Capital explained this week&lt;/a&gt;.&lt;/p&gt;&lt;p&gt;{\"image\":{\"pid\":\"p025p551\"}}&lt;/p&gt;&lt;p&gt;Perhaps the most studied effect concerns the shade&rsquo;s association with desire, seduction, and sin &ndash; a link that can be seen in everything from the &lt;a href=\"http://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Whore_of_Babylon\"&gt;Scarlet Whore of Babylon&lt;/a&gt; to Chris de Burgh&rsquo;s Lady in Red. A string of experiments, by Elliot and other colleagues, have all confirmed that &lt;a href=\"http://www.ncbi.nlm.nih.gov/pubmed/22558822\"&gt;men and women are both rated as being more attractive when wearing red compared to other shades&lt;/a&gt;. Although many of the studies were conducted in the laboratory, with subjects passively rating static photos, it also seems to translate to real-world behaviour; &lt;a href=\"http://www.sciencedaily.com/releases/2012/08/120802111454.htm\"&gt;waitresses in red also tend to get bigger tips from male customers&lt;/a&gt;, for instance; &lt;a href=\"http://onlinelibrary.wiley.com/doi/10.1111/j.1469-8986.2011.01216.x/abstract\"&gt;wearing a red t-shirt can also increase female hitchhikers&rsquo; chances of getting a ride&lt;/a&gt;.&lt;/p&gt;&lt;p&gt;A possible explanation is that slightly redder skin &ndash; thanks to a good circulation &ndash; seems to signal health and fitness; perhaps, by extension, we read the same from the clothes that we wear.&lt;/p&gt;&lt;p&gt;As with the sports results, the secret may lie in the mind of the wearer, as much as the eye of the beholder; in one clever experiment, researchers took mug shots of men and women wearing different clothes. The actual colours were not visible in the photographs, yet somehow, &lt;a href=\"http://www.ncbi.nlm.nih.gov/pubmed/22947805\"&gt;the judges still rated the people wearing red more highly&lt;/a&gt;. &ldquo;They might have put a sexier look on their face or held their posture in a more sexy way,&rdquo; says Elliot.&lt;/p&gt;&lt;p&gt;{\"image\":{\"pid\":\"p025p2qh\"}}&lt;/p&gt;&lt;p&gt;Still, you would do well not to immediately change your wardrobe or paint your office walls. In some contexts, red can provoke other emotions, sometimes undesirable. Perceptions of dominance may contribute to a man&rsquo;s attraction in the clothes he wears, for instance, but it could potentially backfire. &ldquo;If it&rsquo;s a super-bright red it might cue off aggression, which could be negative,&rdquo; says Elliot. And in the exam hall, he has found that &lt;a href=\"http://courseware.eduwest.com/courseware/0111/content/ziyuan/wenxian/01.pdf\"&gt;people perform worse on cognitive tests if they are given to the participant in a red cover slip&lt;/a&gt;.&lt;/p&gt;&lt;p&gt;&lt;strong&gt;Red herrings&lt;/strong&gt;&lt;/p&gt;&lt;p&gt;More importantly, not all the findings of colour psychology are robust enough to be fully trusted just yet. &ldquo;I think the work is at a very early stage of development,&rdquo; says Elliot. The psychologists still need to replicate their results and investigate exactly when the different effects occur, so that we can be sure that the early findings aren&rsquo;t just red herrings. &ldquo;Eventually we might be able to use colour psychology to create a more productive working environment, but we&rsquo;re far from that point. I think it would tremendously premature to regulate what colour sports jerseys could be &ndash; or to outlaw red pens.&rdquo;&lt;/p&gt;&lt;p&gt;Elliot would also like to see more work investigating the rest of the rainbow. He has found that where red may hinder performance, green and blue can &lt;a href=\"http://www.ncbi.nlm.nih.gov/pubmed/22427383\"&gt;encourage creativity in certain kinds of word games&lt;/a&gt;. Even so, Elliot suspects that their influence will be fairly limited, compared to the potent effect that red has over our behaviour.&lt;/p&gt;&lt;p&gt;&ldquo;The perception of red has evolved in such important events and experiences,&rdquo; says Elliot. &ldquo;Red is the colour of ripe fruit, the angry face across from you, the person showing sexual arousal.&rdquo; In this way, it will always be associated with survival, with connotations and influences that run as deep as the blood in our veins. Perhaps we are only confirming what our ancestors realised when they first started painting their bodies: there is no other colour like it.&lt;/p&gt;&lt;p&gt;&lt;em&gt;If you would like to comment on this article, or anything else you have seen on Future, head over to our &lt;/em&gt;&lt;a href=\"https://www.facebook.com/BBCFuture\"&gt;&lt;em&gt;Facebook&lt;/em&gt;&lt;/a&gt;&lt;em&gt; or &lt;/em&gt;&lt;a href=\"https://plus.google.com/u/0/b/107828172298602173375/107828172298602173375/posts\"&gt;&lt;em&gt;Google+&lt;/em&gt;&lt;/a&gt;&lt;em&gt; page, or message us on &lt;/em&gt;&lt;a href=\"https://twitter.com/BBC_Future\"&gt;&lt;em&gt;Twitter&lt;/em&gt;&lt;/a&gt;.&lt;/p&gt;&lt;p style=\"position: relative; display: block; width:100%; padding-bottom:110%\"&gt;&lt;iframe src=\"http://www.bbc.com/future/bespoke/life_in_colour/\" frameborder=\"0\" scrolling=\"no\" style=\"position:absolute;width:100%;height:100%;top:0;left:0;\"&gt;&lt;/iframe&gt;","BusinessUnit":"worldwide","CalloutBody":"","CalloutPosition":"","CalloutSubtitle":"","CalloutTitle":"","Campaign":null,"Collection":[],"DisableAdverts":false,"DisplayDate":"2014-09-01T00:00:00Z","HeadlineLong":"How the colour red warps the mind","HeadlineShort":"Red alert: A mind-warping colour","HideRelated":false,"Intro":"Red is perhaps the most manipulative colour, influencing everything from your behaviour in the workplace to your love life. How? David Robson finds out.\n","IsSyndicated":true,"Location":null,"Partner":null,"PrimaryVertical":"wwfuture","Programme":null,"RelatedStory":[],"RelatedTag":null,"StoryType":"image","SummaryLong":"Red is perhaps the most manipulative colour, influencing everything from your behaviour in the workplace to your love life. How? David Robson finds out.","SummaryShort":"The hidden ways it rules power and sex","Tag":[]},"Metadata":{"CreationDateTime":"2014-11-18T09:13:03Z","Entity":"story","Guid":"21555d2f-386b-4592-841b-a546a80ae32f","Id":"wwfuture/story/20140827-how-the-colour-red-warps-the-mind","ModifiedDateTime":"2014-11-18T09:13:03Z","Project":"wwfuture","Slug":"20140827-how-the-colour-red-warps-the-mind"},"Urn":"urn:pubstack:jative:story:wwfuture/story/20140827-how-the-colour-red-warps-the-mind","_id":"559a563d0b808738c4e2f915"},{"Content":{"AssetCustom":"","AssetIbroadcast":null,"AssetImage":[],"AssetImagePromo":[],"AssetInfographic":"","AssetInline":[],"AssetSelect":"","AssetVideoIb2":null,"AssetVideoMps":null,"Author":[],"BodyHtml":"&lt;p&gt;Imagine the two of us, arm in arm, looking at a sunset, where the horizon is fretted with golden fire and the deep blue night encroaches from the opposite side of the sky. \"What beautiful colours\", I say, and you agree.&lt;/p&gt;&lt;p&gt;And then, in the space of the following silence, I am struck by a worry. I can point at the sky and say it is &lt;a href=\"http://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Blue\"&gt;blue&lt;/a&gt;, and you will concur. But are you &lt;em&gt;really&lt;/em&gt; seeing that blue the way I am seeing it? Perhaps you have just learnt to call what you see \"blue\", but in actual experience you are seeing nothing like the vivid, rich, blue I see. You are an imposter, calling my blue by the same name as yours, but not really seeing it the way I do. Or, even worse, perhaps I am the one seeing a pale imitation blue, while &lt;em&gt;you&lt;/em&gt; see a blue that is infinitely richer and more splendid than mine.&lt;/p&gt;&lt;p&gt;&lt;a href=\"http://www.bbc.co.uk/newsbeat/31655696\"&gt;{\"image\":{\"pid\":\"p02ky5n7\"}}&lt;/a&gt;&lt;/p&gt;&lt;p&gt;&lt;em&gt;&lt;a href=\"http://www.bbc.co.uk/newsbeat/31655696\"&gt;What's behind the debate with #TheDress? BBC Newsbeat explains&lt;/a&gt;&lt;/em&gt;&lt;/p&gt;&lt;p&gt;Now I admit that this worry lies in the realm of philosophy, not neuroscience. You might even ask me why I am worrying about this when we could be enjoying the glorious sunset. But when you think about it, it is not clear that I could ever have direct access to what it is like to be you, and you could never have direct access to what it is like to be me, or someone else, or some&lt;em&gt;thing&lt;/em&gt; else, &lt;a href=\"http://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/What_Is_it_Like_to_Be_a_Bat%3F\"&gt;such as a bat&lt;/a&gt;. My worry seems more plausible when you consider &lt;a href=\"http://www.bbc.co.uk/health/physical_health/conditions/colour_blindness.shtml\"&gt;colour blindness, which affects around 8% of men and half of one percent of women&lt;/a&gt;. Many people do not even realise they are colour blind. They live among the colour-seeing, getting by on the fact that there is usually some other difference between things of different colours that they can use to tell them apart, such as differences in shade or texture.&lt;/p&gt;&lt;p&gt;&lt;strong&gt;How green is my valley?&lt;/strong&gt;&lt;/p&gt;&lt;p&gt;Our colour vision starts with the sensors in the back of the eye that turn light information into electrical signals in the brain &ndash; neuroscientists call them photoreceptors. We have a number of different kinds of these, and most people have three different photoreceptors for coloured light. These are sensitive to blues, greens and reds respectively, and the information is combined to allow us to perceive the full range of colours. Most colour blind men have a weakness in the photoreceptors for green, so they lose a corresponding sensitivity to the shades of green that this variety helps to distinguish.&lt;/p&gt;&lt;p&gt;At the other end of the scale, some people have a particularly heightenedsensitivity to colour. Scientists call these people &lt;a href=\"http://www.perceptionweb.com/abstract.cgi?id=v040075\"&gt;tetrachromats&lt;/a&gt;, meaning &ldquo;four colours&rdquo;, after the four &ndash; rather than three &ndash; colour photoreceptors they possess. Birds and reptiles are tetrachromatic, and this is what allows them to see into the infrared and ultraviolet spectra. Human tetrachromats cannot see beyond the normal visible light spectrum, but instead have an extra photoreceptor that is most sensitive to colour in the scale between red and green, making them more sensitive to all colours within the normal human range. To these individuals, it is the rest of us who are colour blind, as while most of us would be unable to easily distinguish an exact shade of summer-grass-green from Spanish-lime-green, to a tetrachromat it would seem obvious.&lt;/p&gt;&lt;p&gt;&lt;a href=\"http://www.bbc.com/future/story/20140905-the-women-with-super-human-vision\"&gt;{\"image\":{\"pid\":\"p0263325\"}}&lt;/a&gt;&lt;/p&gt;&lt;p&gt;&lt;em&gt;Read more: &lt;a href=\"http://www.bbc.com/future/story/20140905-the-women-with-super-human-vision\"&gt;The tetrachromat women with superhuman vision&lt;/a&gt;&lt;/em&gt;&lt;/p&gt;&lt;p&gt;So yes, as we share this sunset, perhaps I am seeing something you cannot see, or you are seeing something I cannot see. If our colour vision is wired differently, the information going in could be more or less the same between us. But as you tell me this, with the sun sinking slowly below the horizon, you can sense that it has not really helped with my true worry. I am worried &ndash; and perhaps you are too &ndash; that although we both have the same machinery in our eyes and we are both able to see the green of the trees, the red of the sun and the blue of the sky, that when I say \"blue\", it creates an inner experience that differs from yours when you say \"blue\".&lt;/p&gt;&lt;p&gt;&lt;strong&gt;Behind blue eyes&lt;/strong&gt;&lt;/p&gt;&lt;p&gt;My worry about your inner perception of the colour blue is a facet of the basic isolation that is part of the human condition. Even if we think we can really know other people, we cannot be certain of that knowledge. Historically, &lt;a href=\"http://www.simplypsychology.org/behaviourism.html\"&gt;psychologists have adopted a stance called behaviourism&lt;/a&gt;, which acts as if questions about inner experience are irrelevant. This approach states that if you call my blue \"blue\", and you can always tell it from red, and if we both know it is the correct colour for the sky, my eyes and the Smurfs, then who cares what the inner experience is?&lt;/p&gt;&lt;p&gt;There is a lot of mileage in this perspective, but maybe there is also some wisdom in trying to convince ourselves that the difference between our inner experiences is real, and does matter &ndash; and, in fact, that some difference is inevitable. We use common words, and use them to refer to shared experiences, but nobody can see the same sunset, merely because perception is a property of the person, not of the sunset. Because there is something that it is like to be you, and your &ldquo;you-ness&rdquo; is unique, we are certainly seeing different things when we talk about looking at something blue, if only because the act of seeing incorporates feelings and memories, as well as the raw light information arriving at our eyes.&lt;/p&gt;&lt;p&gt;In any case, the sun has set and we walk away. You might be seeing a richer blue in the sunset than me, but you will not have the same memories of the other sunsets I have seen and the people I have watched them with. We could get our vision tested and find out who was better at perceiving colours, but we would never know what it was like to be the other person seeing a particular colour. As long as we can both say that it is a beautiful sunset, we can agree and be secure in the knowledge that I see my blue, and you see your blue, and although we may not see the exact same thing, we have shared it. And that sharing is itself unique to you and me, because no two other people in the world have the same two minds.&lt;/p&gt;&lt;p&gt;&lt;em&gt;If you&rsquo;d like to comment on this story or anything else you have seen on Future, head over to our &lt;a href=\"https://www.facebook.com/BBCFuture\"&gt;Facebook page&lt;/a&gt; or message us on &lt;a href=\"https://twitter.com/#%21/BBC_Future\"&gt;Twitter&lt;/a&gt;. &lt;/em&gt;&lt;/p&gt;","BusinessUnit":"worldwide","CalloutBody":"","CalloutPosition":"","CalloutSubtitle":"","CalloutTitle":"","Campaign":null,"Collection":[],"DisableAdverts":false,"DisplayDate":"2012-02-14T00:00:00Z","Geolocation":null,"HeadlineLong":"Do we all see the same colours?","HeadlineShort":"Do we all see the same colours?","HideRelated":false,"Intro":"Are the colours you see the same as the ones I see? This week, there's been quite the fuss over a notorious dress: nobody can agree on its shade. Our resident psychologist once tackled this strange phenomenon. Here's the science of what's happening in your brain.","IsSyndicated":false,"Location":null,"Partner":null,"PrimaryVertical":"wwfuture","Programme":null,"RelatedStory":[],"RelatedTag":null,"StoryType":"image","SummaryLong":"Are the colours you see the same as the ones I see? Future's resident psychologist has a moment of doubt and wonders if science can help ease his worries. ","SummaryShort":"Intriguing science behind #TheDress","Tag":[]},"Metadata":{"CreationDateTime":"2014-11-18T09:15:29Z","Entity":"story","Guid":"33c07b8b-95ed-43a3-b596-a4586a002674","Id":"wwfuture/story/20120209-do-we-all-see-the-same-colours","ModifiedDateTime":"2014-11-18T09:15:29Z","Project":"wwfuture","Slug":"20120209-do-we-all-see-the-same-colours"},"Urn":"urn:pubstack:jative:story:wwfuture/story/20120209-do-we-all-see-the-same-colours","_id":"559a55e70b808738c4e2edeb"},{"Content":{"AssetCustom":"","AssetIbroadcast":[],"AssetImage":[],"AssetImagePromo":null,"AssetInfographic":"","AssetInline":[],"AssetSelect":"gallery","AssetVideoIb2":null,"AssetVideoMps":null,"Author":[],"BodyHtml":"&lt;p&gt;In the Atacama Desert in northern Chile, it looks as if nothing could ever survive. It is one of the driest places in the world, and some sections of the Mars-like expanse can go 50 years without feeling a drop of rain. As poet Alonso de Ercilla put it in 1569: &ldquo;Towards Atacama, near the deserted coast, you see a land without men, where there is not a bird, not a beast, nor a tree, nor any vegetation.&rdquo;&lt;/p&gt;&lt;p&gt;Yet Atacama is not devoid of life. Microorganisms called endoliths have found a way to cling on, by hiding themselves inside the pores of rocks, where there&rsquo;s just enough water to survive. &ldquo;They support a whole community of organisms that eat the byproducts of their metabolism,&rdquo; says Jocelyne DiRuggiero, a microbiologist at Johns Hopkins University. &ldquo;And they&rsquo;re all just sitting right there in the rocks &ndash; it&rsquo;s quite fascinating.&rdquo;&lt;/p&gt;&lt;p&gt;Life, it seems, has an incredible knack for finding ways to persist. Indeed, microorganisms have been around for nearly four billion years, giving them ample time to adapt to some of the most extreme conditions in the natural world. But are there places left on Earth so harsh that they are rendered sterile?&lt;/p&gt;&lt;p&gt;{\"image\":{\"pid\":\"p01t23kc\"}}&lt;/p&gt;&lt;p&gt;Heat is a good starting point for answering this question. The record for heat tolerance is currently held by a group of organisms called hyperthermophile methanogens, which thrive around the edges of hydrothermal vents in the deep sea. Some of these organisms can grow at &lt;a href=\"http://www.pnas.org/content/105/31/10949.full.pdf\"&gt;temperatures of up to 122C&lt;/a&gt; (252F).&nbsp;&lt;/p&gt;&lt;p&gt;Most researchers believe that around 150C (302F) is the theoretical cut-off point for life, however. At that temperature proteins fall apart and chemical reactions cannot occur &ndash; a quirk of the biochemistry that life on Earth (so far as we know) abides by. This means that microorganisms can thrive around hydrothermal vents, but not directly within them, where temperatures can reach up to 464C (867F). The same is true for the interior of an active volcano on land. &ldquo;I really think temperature is the most hostile parameter,&rdquo; says Helena Santos, a microbial physiologist at the New University of Lisbon and president of the &lt;a href=\"http://extremophiles.org/\"&gt;International Society for Extremophiles&lt;/a&gt;. When things get hot enough, she says, &ldquo;It&rsquo;s impossible &ndash; everything is destroyed.&rdquo;&lt;/p&gt;&lt;p&gt;{\"image\":{\"pid\":\"p01t23bt\"}}&lt;/p&gt;&lt;p&gt;High pressure, by contrast, appears to be less of a problem for life. This means that heat rather than depth probably limits how far below the surface of the Earth life occurs. The centre of the Earth&rsquo;s 6,000C (10,800F) temperature certainly precludes all life, although the depth at which the cut-off occurs is still under investigation. One microorganism called &lt;em&gt;&lt;a href=\"http://www.sciencemag.org/content/322/5899/275\"&gt;Desulforudis audaxviator&lt;/a&gt;&lt;/em&gt; was discovered nearly two miles (3.2km) below the Earth&rsquo;s surface, in a South African gold mine. It has not been in contact with the surface for potentially millions of years, and survives by siphoning nutrients from rocks undergoing radioactive decay.&lt;/p&gt;&lt;p&gt;Life exists at the other extreme, too, in subfreezing conditions. Bacteria in the genus &lt;em&gt;Psychrobacter &lt;/em&gt;can happily live below -10C (14F) in Siberian permafrost and Antarctic glacier mud. &lt;a href=\"http://www.nytimes.com/2013/02/07/science/living-bacteria-found-deep-under-antarctic-ice-scientists-say.html?_r=0\"&gt;Living cells recently turned up&lt;/a&gt; in a subglacial lake below the Antarctic ice. And Antarctica&rsquo;s hypersaline Deep Lake hosts unique &lt;a href=\"http://www.pnas.org/content/early/2013/09/25/1307090110.abstract\"&gt;salt-loving species&lt;/a&gt;, even at -20C (-4F). To survive in these environments, microrganisms possess features such as specially adapted membranes and protein structures, and anti-freeze molecules within their cells. Given that the Earth has been covered in ice multiple times since life first evolved, &ldquo;an ice-covered lake in Antarctica does not seem all that extreme,&rdquo; says Jill Mikucki, a microbiologist at the University of Tennessee.&lt;/p&gt;&lt;p&gt;{\"image\":{\"pid\":\"p01t23nt\"}}&lt;/p&gt;&lt;p&gt;Radiation typically does not deter microorganisms, either. So long as they are not in the direct pathway of an atomic blast &ndash; which would likely burn them up &ndash; they can thrive in containers of radioactive waste or near the epicentre of the Chernobyl disaster, for example. &lt;em&gt;Deinococcus radiodurans&lt;/em&gt;, one of the hardiest of the radio-resistant microorganisms, has &lt;a href=\"http://www.sciencedirect.com/science/article/pii/S0378109702009539\"&gt;survived trips into space&lt;/a&gt; and can endure radiation doses of up to 15,000 gray (the standard measure of the absorbed dose of radiation). For humans, just 5 gray causes death.&lt;/p&gt;&lt;p&gt;Likewise, what we register as deadly chemical environments, some extremophiles call home sweet home. Various organisms depend on arsenic, mercury or other heavy metals for their growth and survival, while others prefer cyanide. In &lt;a href=\"http://www.exploratorium.edu/kamchatka/\"&gt;the hot springs of Kamchatka in Russia&lt;/a&gt;, menageries of microorganisms metabolise using sulphur or carbon monoxide. &ldquo;It&rsquo;s hard to find a chemical that can kill all life,&rdquo; says Frank Robb, a microbiologist at the University of Maryland.&lt;/p&gt;&lt;p&gt;There might be isolated exceptions, however. Don Juan Pond in Antarctica is the saltiest known body of water on the planet, with salinity levels topping 40%. (The Dead Sea is around 33%). Researchers have recovered traces of microbial life from the pond, but they are still trying to determine whether it is actively growing and replicating there, or just blowing in from other locations. Don Juan counts as &ldquo;an example of a place on the surface of Earth where we might expect life, but cannot verify the presence of active life,&rdquo; says Corien Bakermans, a microbiologist at Penn State University.&lt;/p&gt;&lt;p&gt;For now, extreme heat and some synthetic laboratory environments might be the only sterile conditions on the planet&rsquo;s surface that it&rsquo;s possible to find zero traces of life. New organisms are regularly discovered that push the boundaries of life as we understand it, although where that line will ultimately be drawn remains unknown. As Santos says, &ldquo;What does not exist is more difficult to prove than what does exist.&rdquo;&lt;/p&gt;&lt;p&gt;Even if there are some lone holdouts of sterility in the natural world, however, the environment exists in a constant state of flux &ndash; and if extremophiles teach us anything, it is that organisms are always capable of adapting. &ldquo;Give them enough time and they will find a way,&rdquo; says DiRuggiero.&lt;/p&gt;&lt;p&gt;&lt;em&gt;If you would like to comment on this, or anything else you have seen on Future, head over to our &lt;/em&gt;&lt;a href=\"https://www.facebook.com/BBCFuture\"&gt;&lt;em&gt;Facebook&lt;/em&gt;&lt;/a&gt;&lt;em&gt; or &lt;/em&gt;&lt;a href=\"https://plus.google.com/u/0/b/107828172298602173375/107828172298602173375/posts\"&gt;&lt;em&gt;Google+&lt;/em&gt;&lt;/a&gt;&lt;em&gt; page, or message us on &lt;/em&gt;&lt;a href=\"https://twitter.com/BBC_Future\"&gt;&lt;em&gt;Twitter&lt;/em&gt;&lt;/a&gt;&lt;em&gt;.&lt;/em&gt;&lt;/p&gt;","BusinessUnit":"worldwide","CalloutBody":"","CalloutPosition":"","CalloutSubtitle":"","CalloutTitle":"","Campaign":null,"Collection":[],"DisableAdverts":false,"DisplayDate":"2014-03-03T00:00:00Z","HeadlineLong":"The last place on Earth without life","HeadlineShort":"Last place on Earth without life","HideRelated":false,"Intro":"Weird and wonderful creatures can thrive in the most hostile parts on the planet, but there are a few places too harsh for even the hardiest, discovers Rachel Nuwer.                <h1 class="primary-heading" role="heading">
                Should we engineer animals to be smart like humans?
               </h1>
              </div>
             </div>
             <div class="secondary-header-wrapper">
              <div aria-hidden="true" class="bbccom_slot" data-advert-groups="4">
               <div class="bbccom_advert bbccom_display_none">
                <script type="text/javascript">

          <!-- hero-unit end -->
          <div class="page-component-wrapper standard article-content" id="story-content">
           <!-- primary content start -->
           <div class="primary-content" role="main">
            <div class="mpu-wrapper">
             <div aria-hidden="true" class="bbccom_slot" data-advert-groups="1_2_3">
              <div class="bbccom_advert bbccom_display_none">
               <script type="text/javascript">
